Third Division of Possible Things and Critique of Deniers' Proof
Razi defines the third division of possible things and refutes the theologians' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of possible things into three divisions and his critique of the theological argument against non-material entities.

Core Idea
Negative attributes cannot define essence.

Razi introduces the third division of possible things—entities that are neither bodies nor subsisting in bodies. He notes that philosophers affirm such beings while theologians deny them. He then presents the theologians' strongest argument and masterfully refutes it, showing that sharing a negative attribute does not necessitate sharing essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi presents the division, the argument, and his critique.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the completed argument.
2
Identify the third division
Find the definition of the third category of possible things. Note what it is not: neither a body nor inhering in a body.
This is a purely negative definition.
3
Understand the theologians' argument
Trace the logical steps: if such an entity existed, it would equal God in negative attributes → equality in essence → absurdity (the necessary becomes possible or vice versa).
The argument relies on the assumption that sharing negative attributes implies sharing essence.
4
Grasp Razi's critique
Razi points out that equality in a negative attribute (like 'not a body') is trivial; any two distinct essences share all negations of what they are not. Hence the argument fails.
This is a foundational logical point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث من أقسام الممكنات الشيء الذي لا يكون متحيزا ولا يكون قائما بالمتحيز. واعلم أن جمهور الفلاسفة يثبتون هذا القسم وجمهور المتكلمين ينكرونه. وأقوى دلائل المنكرين أن قالوا لو حصل موجود سوى الله تعالى بهذه الصفة لكان ذلك الموجود مساويا لذات الله تعالى في أنه ليس بمتحيز ولا قائم بالمتحيز. ولو حصلت المساواة في هذه الصفة لحصلت المساواة في تمام الماهية ولو حصلت المساواة في تمام الماهية لزم القول بأنه إما أن يكون الواجب ممكنا أو يكون الممكن واجبا وكل ذلك محال. واعلم أن هذه الحجة ضعيفة وذلك لأن الاستواء في كونه ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز استواء في مفهوم سلبي والاستواء في المسلوب لا يوجب الاستواء في الماهية لأن كل ماهيتين مختلفتين فلا بد وأن يتشاركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما وإذا بطلت هذه المقدمة سقطت هذه الحجة. بل نقول الاستواء في الصفات الثبوتية لا يوجب الاستواء في الماهية لأن
The third division of the possible things: the thing that is neither a body (mutahayyiz) nor subsisting in a body. Know that the majority of philosophers affirm this division, and the majority of theologians deny it. The strongest proof of the deniers is that they said: if an existent other than God Most High were to exist with this attribute, that existent would be equal to the essence of God in that it is not a body and not subsisting in a body. If equality in this attribute obtained, equality in the complete essence would obtain. If equality in the complete essence obtained, it would necessarily be said that either the necessary would be possible or the possible would be necessary. All of that is impossible. Know that this argument is weak, because equality in being neither a body nor a state in a body is equality in a negative concept, and equality in a negative [concept] does not necessitate equality in essence. For every two different essences must share in the negation of everything other than them from both. If this premise is invalid, this argument collapses. Rather we say: equality in positive attributes does not necessitate equality in essence, because...
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the third division of possible things: entities that are neither bodies nor inhering in bodies. Razi notes that philosophers affirm such entities (like souls, angels, or intellects) while most theologians deny them. He then gives the strongest argument of the denying theologians: if such an entity existed, it would share with God the attribute of being non-bodily and non-inhering, which would imply equality in essence, leading to absurd consequences (the necessary becoming possible or vice versa). Razi criticizes this argument: equality in negative attributes (i.e., what something is not) does not entail equality in essence, because any two distinct essences will share all negations of what they are not. Thus the argument fails. He begins to add that even equality in positive attributes does not entail equality in essence, but the passage cuts off at the page break.

Key Terms
متحيز
قائم بالمتحيز
مساواة
سلبي
ماهية
واجب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing a negative attribute (like 'not a body') with God implies equality in essence.
Correction: Razi explains that any two different essences share all negations; therefore sharing a negative attribute is trivial and does not entail essential identity.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's critique, why is the theologians' argument that a non-bodily, non-inhering entity would be equal to God in essence invalid?
A) Because such entities do not exist.
B) Because equality in negative attributes does not imply equality in essence.
C) Because God cannot be compared to any other being.
D) Because the theologians should use a different method.

Key Takeaway
Equality in negative attributes does not necessitate equality in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that equality in a negative attribute like 'not being a body' does not make one equal to God in essence. Consider your own spiritual life: do you ever take pride in your conceptual purity about God, thinking that because you reject anthropomorphism, you are somehow closer to Him? How does Razi's point—that all created things share these negations—expose the hidden arrogance in that feeling? What must you now do to ensure your tawhid remains a means of humility and not a badge of superiority?
?
The passage shows that any two different essences necessarily share all negations of what they are not. In your relationship with God, are you more focused on what He is not (e.g., not limited, not a body) than on what He uniquely is (e.g., the Necessary Being, the Creator)? How does this lesson compel you to shift your worship from mere negation to affirmation of His positive attributes?
?
The theologians' argument failed because they treated a shared negative as if it implied shared essence. Think of a time when you mistakenly assumed that because you and another person avoided a particular sin, you were essentially similar in virtue. How does Razi's principle—that shared negatives are trivial—challenge that assumption? What concrete change in how you judge others will you make today?

Refuting the Philosophical Interpretation of Temporal Priority
Imam Razi refutes the philosophers' interpretation of temporal priority for the world's nonexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' interpretation of temporal priority for the nonexistence of the world leads to a contradiction, and how Imam Razi refutes it using a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Temporal priority of nonexistence implies eternal time, motion, and body — contradicting the world's creation.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents an objection from philosophers regarding the meaning of 'the nonexistence of the world is prior to its existence'. After showing that causal and essential priority are not at issue, he focuses on temporal priority. By demonstrating that temporal priority requires eternal time, which in turn requires eternal motion and body, he shows that the philosophers' own interpretation would imply the eternity of the world, thus refuting their position. Let's examine this logical argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the objection
The philosophers question what is meant by saying the world's nonexistence is prior to its existence. Recognize that they are not denying priority but interpreting it.
The philosophers are engaging with the claim made by theologians about the world's createdness.
2
Follow the elimination of possible meanings
Notice how Imam Razi systematically eliminates causal and essential priority as intended meanings, leaving only temporal priority.
Causal priority is impossible because nonexistence cannot cause existence; essential priority is agreed but does not imply temporal beginning.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Trace the argument: if temporal priority is assumed, then time must be eternal, leading to eternal motion and body, which contradicts the claim that the world is originated. This shows the interpretation is self-defeating.
The argument uses the philosophers' own assumptions about time and motion to defeat their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تلخصت هذه المقدمة، فعند ذلك قالت الفلاسفة ما المراد من قولكم : عدم العالم متقدم على وجوده؟ لا جائز أن يكون المراد منه التقدم بالعلية والتأثير؛ لأن العدم لا يكون علة للوجود، ولأن العلة يجب أن تكون حاصلة على المعلول. فلو كان عدم العالم علة لوجوده لزم أن يحصل عدمه ووجوده معا. وهذا محال. وأما إن كان المراد منه التقدم بالذات. فهذا متفق عليه؛ وذلك لأن العالم ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته يستحق لذاته أن لا يستحق الوجود وصيرورته مستحقا للوجود ، إنما يكون من غيره وما بالذات قبل ما بالغير. فإذن عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية بالذات بالاتفاق ولا جائز أن يكون المراد منه التقدم بالشرف، ولا بالمكان، وهو ظاهر. بقي أن يكون تقدم عدمه على وجوده تقدمًا بالزمان ولكنا بينا أن التقدم بالزمان لا يتقرر إلا عند حصول الزمان. فلو كان عدم العالم متقدمًا على وجوده بالزمان تقدما من الأزل إلى الأبد لزم أن يكون الزمان موجودًا من الأزل إلى الأبد. لكن الزمان من لواحق الحركة، التي هي من لواحق الجسم فيلزم من تفسير المحدث بما ذكرتم القول بقدم الزمان والحركة والجسم، وذلك نقيض مطلوبكم، وضد غرضكم، فثبت أن القول بتفسير كون العالم محدثًا بما ذكرتم، يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه . فوجب - يكون هذا التفسير باطلاً.
Now that this preamble is summarized, the philosophers said: 'What is meant by your statement “the nonexistence of the world is prior to its existence”? It cannot be intended as priority by causality and influence, because nonexistence is not a cause of existence, and because the cause must coexist with the effect. If the nonexistence of the world were a cause of its existence, then its nonexistence and existence would have to occur together, which is impossible. If it is intended as priority by essence, this is agreed upon, because the world is possible in itself, and that which is possible in itself deserves in itself not to deserve existence, and its becoming deserving of existence is only from another, and what is by essence is prior to what is by another. So its nonexistence is prior to its existence by essential priority by consensus. And it cannot be intended as priority by honor or by place, which is obvious. It remains that the priority of its nonexistence over its existence is temporal priority, but we have shown that temporal priority is only established with the existence of time. So if the nonexistence of the world were temporally prior to its existence with a priority from eternity to eternity, then time would have to exist from eternity to eternity. But time is an accident of motion, which is an accident of body. Thus, from the interpretation of the originated (al-muhdath) as you have mentioned, it follows that time, motion, and body are eternal, which is contrary to your claim and opposite to your purpose. So it is established that the statement interpreting the world as originated in the way you have mentioned leads its affirmation to its negation. Therefore, this interpretation must be false.'
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by philosophers regarding the meaning of 'the nonexistence of the world is prior to its existence' and an analysis of possible interpretations. The philosophers argue that causal priority is impossible because nonexistence cannot be a cause. Essential priority is agreed upon but does not imply temporal beginning. Honorific and locative priority are irrelevant. The only remaining possibility is temporal priority. But temporal priority requires time; if the world's nonexistence is eternal, then time must be eternal, leading to the eternity of time, motion, and body—which contradicts the original claim that the world is created. Therefore, the interpretation defeats itself. The argument is structured as a reduction ad absurdum: assuming the philosophers' definition leads to consequences that negate their own position.

Key Terms
عدم (nonexistence)
تقدم بالعلية (causal priority)
تقدم بالذات (essential priority)
زمان (time)
حركة (motion)
جسم (body)
محدث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority is a valid interpretation of the priority of nonexistence.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that temporal priority leads to the eternity of time, motion, and body, which contradicts the claim that the world is created, making the interpretation self-defeating.

Checkpoint
Why is causal priority rejected for the priority of nonexistence over existence?
A) Because cause must be prior to effect
B) Because nonexistence cannot be a cause
C) Because it is agreed upon
D) Because it leads to temporal priority

Key Takeaway
The interpretation that the world's nonexistence is temporally prior to its existence leads to the conclusion that time, motion, and body are eternal, which contradicts the very claim that the world is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers fell into error by trying to fit God's creation into a human conceptual framework (time). Examine your own life: where have you unconsciously imposed your limited human categories onto the Divine? What practical steps can you take to humble your mind before Allah's transcendence?
?
Imam Razi shows that even a seemingly subtle misinterpretation leads to a logical contradiction. Are there beliefs you hold that, upon honest reflection, conflict with each other? Are you willing to abandon any of them for the sake of truth, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The rejected interpretation would imply the eternity of the world, which contradicts the Quranic teaching of creation. When you recite verses about creation, do you truly internalize that the universe came into existence from nothing by Allah's will, or do you subconsciously assume some eternal substrate? How can you deepen your conviction of tawheed in this regard?

The Impossibility of Defining Muhdath as Preceded by Another
Elimination and reductio against a second definition of muhdath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining the originated (muhdath) as 'that which is preceded by another' leads to impossibility and contradiction, and how the author eliminates alternative interpretations of precedence.

Core Idea
Defining muhdath as 'preceded by another' reduces to temporal priority, which is impossible.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi continues his critique of alternative definitions of the originated (muhdath). He examines the second definition: 'the originated is that which is preceded by another.' By analyzing each possible meaning of 'precedence,' he shows that only temporal precedence remains, but that leads to absurd consequences. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the author methodically examines each type of precedence.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the four types of precedence
The author considers causal, essential/honorific, locative, and temporal precedence. Find where each is mentioned.
These are the possible meanings of 'sabq' (precedence).
3
Understand why each non-temporal type fails
For causal, essential, and honorific, the author says they are 'agreed upon' but do not imply a beginning. Locative is negated by consensus. This leaves only temporal.
4
Grasp the two reasons temporal precedence is impossible
One: it makes God temporal. Two: it makes time temporal. Both are impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
5
See the contradiction if assumed
Even if assumed valid, it leads to an infinite chain of time, motion, and body, contradicting the claim that the world has a beginning.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التفسير الثاني : وهو أن يقال : المحدث ما يكون مسبوقا بالغير. فنقل إما أن يكون المراد بهذا السبق السبق بالعلية . فذاك متفق عليه ؛ لأن مذهبت المقدمة الثانية العالم ممكن لذاته، واجب لوجوب علته . لكن هذا لا يقتضي أن يكون للحوادث بداية ؛ لأنه لا يمتنع في العقل دوام المعلول بدوام علته وإن كان المراد بهذا السبق هو السبق بالذات أو بالشرف فهو أيضًا متفق عليه. وأما السبق بالمكان فهو منفي بالاتفاق وعلى تقدير ثبوته، فذلك لا يوجب أن يكون للحوادث بداية، إذ لا يمتنع في بداهة العقول وجود موجود فوق العالم، ويكونان موجودين أزلا وأبدًا . فلم يبق ههنا إلا أن يفسر ذلك السبق بالسبق الزماني. وهذا محال. وبتقدير الصحة فهو متناقض. أما أنه محال، فلوجهين أحدهما : أنه يلزم منه كون الله تعالى زمانيا، وذلك محال. وأما ثانيا : فيلزم منه كون الزمان زمانيًا . وذلك أيضًا محال. وأما بتقدير الصحة، فهو متناقض ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان الله تعالى متقدمًا على العالم تقدما لا أول له وذلك التقدم تقدم زماني، لزم إثبات زمان لا أول له . وحينئذ يعود الكلام إلى أنه يلزم قدم الزمان والحركة والجسم. فهذا تمام تقرير هذا الإشكال.
As for the second interpretation: that it is said: 'The originated (al-muhdath) is that which is preceded by another.' Then we say: Either what is meant by this precedence is precedence by causality—that is agreed upon because our doctrine... (second preamble): The world is possible in itself, necessary due to its cause. But this does not require that events have a beginning, because it is not rationally impossible for the effect to continue with the continuation of its cause. If what is meant by this precedence is precedence by essence or by honor, that also is agreed upon. As for precedence by place, it is negated by consensus, and even if it were affirmed, that would not necessitate a beginning for events, since it is not precluded by the innate reason that there exists an existent above the world, and the two exist eternally and forever. So nothing remains here except to interpret that precedence as temporal precedence. And this is impossible. And if we assume its validity, it is contradictory. As for its impossibility, it is for two reasons: one: it entails that God (exalted) is temporal (zamani), which is impossible. And second: it entails that time itself is temporal, which is also impossible. As for its being contradictory if assumed: because if God (exalted) is prior to the world with a priority that has no beginning, and that priority is temporal, then it necessitates the affirmation of time with no beginning. Then the argument returns to the same point: that it necessitates the eternity of time, motion, and body. This is the complete presentation of this objection.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage analyzes the second definition of 'muhdath' (the originated) as 'that which is preceded by another'. The author examines possible meanings of 'precedence' (al-sabq). If it means causal precedence, it is agreed upon but does not imply a beginning. If essential or honorific precedence, also agreed. If locative precedence, it is negated. Therefore, only temporal precedence remains, but that is impossible for two reasons: (1) it would make God temporal, and (2) it would make time temporal. Additionally, if assumed valid, it leads to an infinite temporal regress and the eternity of time, motion, and body, contradicting the original claim. The argument proceeds by elimination: each possible interpretation is considered and deemed insufficient or impossible, leaving no valid interpretation. The 'second preamble' (المقدمة الثانية) reaffirms that the world is possible in itself but necessary through its cause, which does not require a temporal beginning.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdath)
سبق (precedence)
علية (causality)
ذات (essence)
شرف (honor)
مكان (place)
زماني (temporal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The originated can be defined as preceded by another without implying temporal priority.
Correction: The author shows that all non-temporal interpretations either do not imply a beginning or are negated, so only temporal remains, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the first reason why interpreting precedence as temporal is impossible?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of times
B) It makes God temporal
C) It makes time temporal
D) It contradicts the consensus on precedence by place

Key Takeaway
The definition of muhdath as 'preceded by another' ultimately requires temporal priority, which leads to absurdities, so it is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not temporal, what does that mean for how you approach Him in your daily life? Are you treating God as if He is bound by time, expecting immediate results? Reflect on how the timelessness of God should change your patience and trust.
?
The argument shows that reasoning about God's attributes must avoid contradictions. How have you, like the people in this passage, tried to define God in ways that actually reduce Him to something temporal? Have you ever imagined God as existing 'before' the world in a temporal sense? How does this lesson correct that?
?
The author eliminates all non-temporal interpretations, ultimately forcing a choice. In your own life, do you avoid confronting the difficult implications of your beliefs? Are you content with superficial understandings of God's nature? Commit to studying deeper.

Definition of Originated and the First Proof
Razi defines 'originated' and gives the first reason that possibility has no beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of the 'originated' and his first rational argument that the possibility of occurrence has no beginning.

Core Idea
If possibility had a beginning, it would require contradictory essential changes.

Razi begins by defining the term 'originated' and setting up the problem: does the possibility of events have a beginning? He declares the first option false and provides three proofs. This lesson covers the definition and the first proof, which argues that if possibility had a first moment, it would imply a change from essential impossibility to possibility, undermining all rational certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and statement
Read the first part where Razi defines 'originated' and presents the two alternatives for the validity of occurrence.
Notice that he immediately states the first alternative is false.
2
Analyze the first proof
Focus on the conditional reasoning: if validity had a beginning, what must have been before it? Follow the two sub-cases.
The proof relies on the immutability of essential properties.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why allowing a change in essential modality would destroy all rational certainty.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في تفسير المحدث: وهو أن صحة حدوث الحوادث إما أن تكون لها بداية، أو لا تكون. والقسم الأول باطل. لوجوه: المبدأ.
أحدها: أنه لو كان للصحة أول لما كانت الصحة الذاتية حاصلة قبل ذلك وإذا لم تكن الصحة الذاتية حاصلة قبل ذلك المبدأ، فالحاصل قبل ذلك المبدأ إما الوجوب الذاتي، أو الامتناع الذاتي. فإن كان الأول كان القول بقدم العالم ألزم. وإن كان الثاني فالعالم قبل ذلك المبدأ كان ممتنعًا لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته. وهذا محال . لأن الأمور الذاتية لا تتقلب البتة . ولو جوزنا ذلك، فحينئذ لا يبقى الوثوق بحكم العقل في جواز الجائزات ووجوب الواجبات وامتناع الممتنعات. ولعل سائر الممتنعات لذواتها تنقلب واجبة لذواتها أو بالضد. وكل ذلك باطل .
وثانيهما: وهو أنا لو قلنا بأنه كان ممتنعا لذاته ثم انقلب ممكنا . فحدوث ذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون لأمر، أو لا لأمر، فإن كان لأمر فحدوث ذلك الإمكان يكون ممكنا لذاته والكلام في الإمكان الثاني، كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل. وإن كان لا لأمر، فقد جوزتم أن ما كان ممتنعا لذاته انقلب ممكنا لذاته، لا لأمر. والإمكان للممكن واجب. فحصول ذلك الإمكان كان ممتنعا لذاته قبل ذلك المبدأ، ثم صار واجبًا لذاته. وإذا جاز ذلك، فلِمَ لا يجوز في وجود المحدثات أن يقال : إنه كان ممتنعا لذاته، ثم انقلب واجبًا لذاته؟ وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع. وهو محال.
وثالثهما : أن حدوث ذلك الإمكان. إما أن يقال : إنه كان موقوفا على حدوث أمر ما، بأن يوجد شيء بعد عدمه، أو بأن يعدم شيء بعد وجوده، أو لم يكن موقوفا على ذلك. فإن كان الأول كان الكلام في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بحدوث ذلك الأمر، كما في الأول فيلزم التسلسل . وإن كان الثاني لم يكن اختصاص حدوث ذلك الإمكان بذلك الوقت أولى من اختصاصه بسائر الأوقات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن لصحة حدوث الحوادث بداية. وكذلك القول في بيان أنه لا بداية لصحة تأثير المؤثر في إحداث المحدثات.
The second discussion on the interpretation of the originated (al-muhdath): That the validity (sihha) of the occurrence of events (huḍūth al-ḥawādith) either has a beginning, or does not. The first division is invalid, for reasons: the beginning. One of them: that if validity had a first, then essential validity would not be present before that. And if essential validity is not present before that beginning, then what is present before that beginning is either essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī) or essential impossibility (al-imtināʿ al-dhātī). If the first, then the statement of the pre-eternity of the world would be more necessary. And if the second, then the world before that beginning was impossible in itself, then became possible in itself. This is impossible, because essential matters never change. And if we allowed that, then no confidence would remain in the judgment of reason concerning the permissibility of permissible things, the necessity of necessary things, and the impossibility of impossible things. Perhaps other things impossible in themselves would change to necessary in themselves or the opposite. All of that is false. The second: if we say that it was impossible in itself then became possible, then the occurrence of that possibility either is due to something or not. If due to something, then the occurrence of that possibility is possible in itself, and the discussion about the second possibility is like the first, leading to infinite regress. If not due to something, then you have allowed that what was impossible in itself became possible in itself without cause. And possibility (imkān) for the possible is necessary. So the occurrence of that possibility was impossible in itself before that beginning, then became necessary in itself. And if that is allowed, then why not say concerning the existence of originated things: it was impossible in itself, then became necessary in itself? And that would entail the negation of the Maker (nāfī al-ṣāniʿ). This is impossible. The third: the occurrence of that possibility either is stated to be dependent on the occurrence of some matter, such that something exists after its non-existence, or something ceases after its existence, or not dependent on that. If the first, then the discussion concerns the specification of that time by the occurrence of that matter, as in the first, leading to infinite regress. And if the second, then the specification of the occurrence of that possibility to that time is not more fitting than its specification to other times. So it is established by these proofs that it cannot be said that the validity of the occurrence of events has a beginning. And likewise concerning the exposition that there is no beginning for the validity of the effect of the efficient cause (taʾthīr al-muʾaththir) in bringing about originated things.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Razi defines the originated (al-muhdath) and states that the validity (sihha) of the occurrence of events either has a beginning or does not. He then declares the first division false and provides three reasons (wujuh). The first reason argues that if validity had a first moment, then before that moment the thing would be either essentially necessary or essentially impossible. If it were essentially necessary, that would make the world pre-eternal. If it were essentially impossible, then it would have changed from impossible to possible, which is impossible because essential properties do not change, and if they did, no rational certainty would remain. The second reason argues that if possibility emerged from impossibility, that emergence either has a cause (leading to infinite regress) or is uncaused (allowing any impossible to become necessary, leading to atheism). The third reason argues that if the emergence of possibility depends on a prior event, that leads to regress; if not, the specification to a particular time is arbitrary. The conclusion is that the validity of occurrence has no beginning, and likewise the effect of the efficient cause in bringing about originated things has no beginning.

Key Terms
المحدث
صحة الحدوث
الوجوب الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of events could have a temporal beginning.
Correction: Razi shows that a beginning would require a change in essential properties, which is impossible, so possibility cannot have a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if before the beginning the world was essentially impossible, what would that entail?
A) The world must be pre-eternal.
B) The impossible became possible, which is a change in essential properties.
C) The world was necessarily existent.
D) The possibility was uncaused.

Key Takeaway
The validity of occurrence cannot have a beginning because that would require an impossible change in essential modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the validity of occurrence had a beginning, it would mean that before that moment the world was either necessary or impossible. Which of these two options shakes your confidence in the stability of reality? How does knowing that essential properties cannot change affect your trust in the consistency of Allah's creation?
?
This proof shows that rational certainty depends on the immutability of essences. When you face doubts about your faith, do you ever question whether the basic principles of reasoning (like impossibility of contradiction) still hold? What would it mean for your deen if essential impossibilities could become possible?
?
The argument concludes that possibility (sihhat al-huduth) is eternal. Do you live as if the possibility of good deeds—like repentance or seeking knowledge—is always available to you, or do you sometimes act as if it has a deadline? How does the eternity of possibility in Allah's decree challenge your procrastination in turning to Him?

The Second Proof: Avoiding Regress and Atheism
Razi gives the second reason that possibility has no beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that if possibility emerged from impossibility, it must be caused, leading to infinite regress, or uncaused, leading to atheism.

Core Idea
If possibility arises from impossibility, it must have a cause or not; both lead to absurdity.

In the second proof, Razi considers the scenario where possibility emerges from an earlier state of impossibility. He examines whether that emergence has a cause. If it does, the same question applies to the cause's own possibility, leading to infinite regress. If it does not, then an uncaused change from impossibility to possibility is allowed, which would open the door to atheism. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Razi assumes for argument that the world was impossible in itself and then became possible. Focus on the transition.
This is a hypothetical scenario to test the idea of a beginning.
2
Follow the two branches
The emergence of possibility either has a cause or does not. Trace each branch carefully.
The first branch leads to infinite regress; the second leads to negating the Creator.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Recognize that both outcomes are impossible, so the original scenario cannot be true.
This strengthens the case that possibility has no beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني في تفسير المحدث: وهو أن صحة حدوث الحوادث إما أن تكون لها بداية، أو لا تكون. والقسم الأول باطل. لوجوه: المبدأ.
أحدها: أنه لو كان للصحة أول لما كانت الصحة الذاتية حاصلة قبل ذلك وإذا لم تكن الصحة الذاتية حاصلة قبل ذلك المبدأ، فالحاصل قبل ذلك المبدأ إما الوجوب الذاتي، أو الامتناع الذاتي. فإن كان الأول كان القول بقدم العالم ألزم. وإن كان الثاني فالعالم قبل ذلك المبدأ كان ممتنعًا لذاته، ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته. وهذا محال . لأن الأمور الذاتية لا تتقلب البتة . ولو جوزنا ذلك، فحينئذ لا يبقى الوثوق بحكم العقل في جواز الجائزات ووجوب الواجبات وامتناع الممتنعات. ولعل سائر الممتنعات لذواتها تنقلب واجبة لذواتها أو بالضد. وكل ذلك باطل .
وثانيهما: وهو أنا لو قلنا بأنه كان ممتنعا لذاته ثم انقلب ممكنا . فحدوث ذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون لأمر، أو لا لأمر، فإن كان لأمر فحدوث ذلك الإمكان يكون ممكنا لذاته والكلام في الإمكان الثاني، كما في الأول، فيلزم التسلسل. وإن كان لا لأمر، فقد جوزتم أن ما كان ممتنعا لذاته انقلب ممكنا لذاته، لا لأمر. والإمكان للممكن واجب. فحصول ذلك الإمكان كان ممتنعا لذاته قبل ذلك المبدأ، ثم صار واجبًا لذاته. وإذا جاز ذلك، فلِمَ لا يجوز في وجود المحدثات أن يقال : إنه كان ممتنعا لذاته، ثم انقلب واجبًا لذاته؟ وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع. وهو محال.
وثالثهما : أن حدوث ذلك الإمكان. إما أن يقال : إنه كان موقوفا على حدوث أمر ما، بأن يوجد شيء بعد عدمه، أو بأن يعدم شيء بعد وجوده، أو لم يكن موقوفا على ذلك. فإن كان الأول كان الكلام في اختصاص ذلك الوقت بحدوث ذلك الأمر، كما في الأول فيلزم التسلسل . وإن كان الثاني لم يكن اختصاص حدوث ذلك الإمكان بذلك الوقت أولى من اختصاصه بسائر الأوقات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن لصحة حدوث الحوادث بداية. وكذلك القول في بيان أنه لا بداية لصحة تأثير المؤثر في إحداث المحدثات.

?

Are you living today as though you are self-sufficient and independent? What would it mean to truly live as a dependent being this very hour?
?
Razi warns that allowing an uncaused transition leads to atheism. Is there any area of your life where you act as if things happen without a cause or purpose, ignoring Allah's hand? What would change if you saw every event as caused by Him?
?
If the proof forces you to accept that the world's possibility requires a cause, then your own existence requires a cause right now. Are you actively acknowledging that every breath is given by Allah? What can you do in the next five minutes to remind yourself of this dependence?

The Third Proof: Arbitrariness and the Conclusion
Razi gives the third reason and concludes the discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that the beginning of possibility would lead to either infinite regress or arbitrary temporal specification, and the conclusion that possibility has no beginning.

Core Idea
If possibility had a beginning, its timing would be either dependent or arbitrary—both impossible.

In the third proof, Razi examines whether the emergence of possibility depends on a prior event or not. If dependent, we ask about the time of that prior event, leading to regress. If independent, there is no reason to choose one time over another, making the beginning arbitrary. The lesson also covers the final conclusion that the validity of occurrence has no beginning, and likewise the efficacy of the Creator in bringing about originated things has no beginning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third alternative
Razi considers whether the emergence of possibility depends on some prior event (existence or ceasing of something) or not.
This is a different angle from the previous proofs.
2
Analyze the two sub-options
If dependent, infinite regress; if independent, arbitrary timing.
Both are unacceptable.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final conclusion that possibility has no beginning and similarly the efficacy of the efficient cause has no beginning.
This ties the argument back to the divine attribute of power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Objection: Does the Proof Lead to Pre-Eternity? Razi's Answer
Razi raises an objection and provides an answer to protect the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's objection that the proof of contingency might imply the world's pre-eternity, and how he resolves it by distinguishing between the general validity of occurrence and the specific condition of being preceded.

Core Idea
What has no beginning in possibility may still be impossible to be eternal if it is defined by precedence.

Razi anticipates a powerful objection: if the validity of events occurring has no beginning, then the world could be pre-eternal, which would contradict the impossibility of God's power creating in pre-eternity. He then answers by clarifying that the particular originated thing, defined as 'preceded by another,' cannot be pre-eternal by definition. This distinction saves the proof from implying the world's pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Razi presents the objection. Note how he argues that if the validity of occurrence has no beginning, then the world's existence in pre-eternity becomes possible, leading to a contradiction about God's power.
The objection hinges on the concept of 'combination of contradictories'.
2
Identify the contradiction
The objection claims that saying 'the world cannot be pre-eternal' and 'the world is possible in pre-eternity' are contradictory. Understand why this would be a problem if both are asserted about the same thing at the same time.
3
Study the answer
Read Razi's answer carefully. He distinguishes between the general statement about the validity of occurrence and the particular originated thing defined by being preceded. Notice that even if validity has no beginning, a thing defined as preceded cannot be pre-eternal.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت ذلك كان كون العالم موجودًا في الأزل غير ممتنع . وكذلك أيضًا يكون تأثير المؤثر في وجود العالم أزلا غير ممتنع. وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بأنه يمتنع كون العالم أزليّا، ويمتنع تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في إيجاد العالم في الأزل : جمعا بين الحكم بحصول الامتناع في الأزل وبحصول الجواز في الأزل. وذلك جمع بين النقيضين. وهو محال. وهذا تمام الإشكال.
والجواب عنه : أنه لا يلزم من قولنا : إنه لا بداية لصحة حدوث الحوادث : أن يحكم بجواز كون العالم أزليّا . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا أخذنا هذا الحادث المعين بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالغير، فهذا الحادث المأخوذ مع هذا الشرط، إما أن تكون لصحة حدوثه بداية وإما أن لا تكون لها بداية والأول باطل، لجميع الدلائل التي ذكرتموها. فبقي أنه لا بداية لصحة حدوث هذا الحادث المأخوذ مع هذا الشرط. ثم إنا نعلم قطعًا امتناع كون هذا الحادث أزليّا ; لأن الأزل عبارة عن عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وهذا الحادث مأخوذ بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالغير. والجمع بينهما محال. فثبت أن القول بأنه لا مبدأ لصحة حدوث الحوادث لا يقتضي القول بجواز كون ذلك الشيء أزليا.
And when that is established, then the existence of the world in pre-eternity (azal) is not impossible. Likewise the effect of the efficient cause in the existence of the world in pre-eternity is not impossible. And when this is established, then the statement that the world's being pre-eternal is impossible, and that the effect of God's power (qudra) in bringing the world into existence in pre-eternity is impossible, would be a combination of the judgment of impossibility in pre-eternity and the judgment of possibility in pre-eternity. That is a combination of contradictories (jamʿ bayn al-naqīḍayn). This is impossible. This completes the objection. The answer to it: that our statement that there is no beginning for the validity of the occurrence of events does not entail the judgment that the world is possible to be pre-eternal. The proof of that: if we take this particular originated thing (hādhā al-ḥādith al-muʿayyan) with the condition of its being preceded by another (musbiqan bi al-ghayr), then this originated thing taken with this condition, either the validity of its occurrence has a beginning or not. The first is false, by all the proofs you mentioned. So it remains that there is no beginning for the validity of occurrence of this originated thing taken with this condition. Then we know with certainty the impossibility of this originated thing being pre-eternal, because pre-eternity (azal) is the expression of not being preceded by another (ʿadam al-musbiqiyya bi al-ghayr), and this originated thing is taken with the condition of being preceded by another. The combination of the two is impossible. So it is established that the statement that there is no beginning for the validity of occurrence of events does not entail the judgment that thing is possible to be pre-eternal.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that if the validity of occurrence has no beginning, then the world's existence in pre-eternity becomes possible, which would contradict the claim that God's power to create in pre-eternity is impossible—thus combining possibility and impossibility in pre-eternity, a contradiction. Razi's answer is that the objection confuses two things: the general statement about the validity of occurrence and the particular originated thing that is defined by being preceded by another. When we consider any particular originated thing (like this world) with the condition that it is preceded by another, its validity of occurrence indeed has no beginning, but it cannot be pre-eternal because pre-eternity means not being preceded, which contradicts its defining condition. Thus the proof does not lead to the world's pre-eternity.

Key Terms
الأزل
المسبوقية بالغير
الجمع بين النقيضين
الحادث المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if possibility has no beginning, then any particular thing could be eternal.
Correction: But Razi shows that a thing defined as 'preceded by another' cannot be pre-eternal, even if its possibility has no beginning.

Checkpoint
Why, according to Razi's answer, does the lack of a beginning for the validity of occurrence not imply that the world is pre-eternal?
A) Because the world is essentially impossible in pre-eternity.
B) Because the particular originated thing is taken with the condition of being preceded, which contradicts pre-eternity.
C) Because God's power is limited to creation in time.
D) Because the proofs only apply to non-physical things.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the particular originated thing's condition of being preceded prevents it from being pre-eternal, even if the possibility of its occurrence has no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's answer hinges on a precise distinction between a general statement and a particular conditioned one. Think of a belief you hold about Allah's power that might be based on a general principle without considering specific conditions. Are you willing to re-examine that belief with the same rigor Razi shows?
?
The objection seemed convincing until Razi pointed out the hidden assumption about the condition of being preceded. When have you been persuaded by an argument that sounded logical but later discovered it relied on a confusion? What does this teach you about guarding your heart against hasty conclusions in matters of faith?
?
If the precision of theological reasoning can prevent contradictions about Allah's attributes, how much more should you be careful in the way you speak about your own relationship with Allah? What condition in your life might you be ignoring that prevents you from seeing the truth clearly?

The Five Possible Positions on the Creation of Bodies
Al-Razi enumerates the five possible positions on the creation or eternity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi enumerates the five logically possible positions regarding whether bodies are created or eternal.

Core Idea
All possible views on bodies' origin reduce to just five logical combinations.

Al-Razi begins his third introduction by clarifying the playing field. Before arguing for any position, he shows that the debate is limited to five and only five logical possibilities. This enumeration is essential for systematic theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the five positions
The passage lists five positions based on two categories: essence (ذوات) and attributes (صفات). Find each combination and the option of suspension.
3
Ask: why only five?
Reflect on why these five exhaust all logical possibilities. This is a foundational step for subsequent arguments.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة: في شرح مذاهب الناس في هذه المسألة ·
المذاهب الممكنة في هذه المسألة لا تزيد على خمسة، لأنه إما أن يقال: الأجسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها أو يقال إنها قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها. أو يقال: إنها قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها. أو يقال إنها محدثة بذواتها قديمة بصفاتها. أو يتوقف في كل واحد من هذه الأقسام.
The Third Introduction: Explaining the schools of people on this issue.
The possible schools on this issue do not exceed five, because either it is said: bodies are originated in their essences and attributes, or it is said they are eternal in their essences and attributes, or it is said: they are eternal in their essences, originated in their attributes, or it is said: they are originated in their essences, eternal in their attributes, or one suspends judgment on each one of these categories.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a classification: Al-Razi introduces the third introductory section by stating that the possible positions on the issue (presumably the creation or eternity of bodies) are limited to five. He lists them in binary terms: either bodies are originated (ḥāditha) in both essence and accidents, eternal in both, eternal in essence but originated in accidents, originated in essence but eternal in accidents, or one suspends judgment. This enumeration is exhaustive for the logical possibilities based on the two categories of essence (dhāt) and attributes (ṣifāt).

Key Terms
الأجسام (al-ajsām, bodies)
محدثة (ḥāditha, originated/created)
قديمة (qadīma, eternal)
ذوات (dhawāt, essences)
صفات (ṣifāt, attributes)
توقف (tawaqquf, suspension of judgment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students often think Al-Razi is endorsing one of these positions here.
Correction: Al-Razi is merely mapping the logical possibilities; he does not adopt any view at this stage.

Checkpoint
How many possible positions does Al-Razi list on the issue of bodies?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
There are exactly five logically possible positions on whether bodies are created or eternal, based on combinations of essence and attributes, plus suspension of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something about the physical world must either be fully created or fully eternal, without considering the possibility of mixed origins? How would you feel if that assumption were wrong?
?
Do you suspend judgment on matters you haven't fully investigated, or do you feel pressure to take a position even when you lack evidence? How does this lesson's inclusion of suspension challenge your tendency?
?
This classification is purely logical. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs about the origin of the world with the same rigorous honesty, even if it leads to uncomfortable uncertainty? Will you take time today to reflect on one belief you hold unexamined?

The Aristotelian View: Bodies Eternal in Essence and Accidents
Al-Razi presents the Aristotelian view of eternal bodies, distinguishing celestial and elemental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian position that bodies are eternal in essence and accidents, with a distinction between celestial and elemental bodies.

Core Idea
Aristotle held that the universe has no temporal beginning: matter and form are eternal in succession.

Al-Razi introduces the second of several possibilities regarding the origin of bodies: the view of Aristotle and his followers. According to this view, bodies are eternal both in essence and in their attributes, but with a key distinction between celestial and sublunary realms. Before we examine the critiques, we need to grasp what this position actually claims.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall claim
Read the passage and identify that this is the second possibility: bodies are eternal in essence and attributes. Note that it is attributed to 'some philosophers' and specifically Aristotle.
2
Differentiate celestial vs elemental bodies
Notice the two categories: celestial bodies are eternal in essence and determinate attributes except motions; elemental bodies have eternal matter but forms and accidents are in an endless chain.
Celestial bodies have a different kind of eternity than elemental ones.
3
Identify the exception for motions
See that celestial bodies' motions are not eternal in a beginningless sense; each motion is preceded by another without beginning (infinite regress).
4
Connect to the broader theological discussion
Ask why this view matters for Islamic theology. Al-Razi is laying out the views that later will be refuted from a Muslim perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثاني: وهو القول بأن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها. فهو قول بعض الفلاسفة وتفصيل مذاهبهم : أن الأجسام الفلكية قديمة بذواتها وقديمة بصفاتها المعينة. إلا حركاتها . فإن كل واحدة من حركاتها مسبوقة بحركة أخرى، لا إلى بداية . وأما الأجسام العنصرية. فإن هيولاها قديمة وأما صورها وأعراضها فكل واحد منها مسبوق بآخر، لا إلى بداية. وهذا مذهب (أرسطاطاليس) وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين.
And as for the second possibility: it is the statement that bodies are eternal in their essences and attributes. So it is the statement of some of the philosophers. And the detail of their schools: that celestial bodies are eternal in their essences and eternal in their determinate attributes, except their motions, for each one of their motions is preceded by another motion, without beginning. And as for elemental bodies, their matter (hayūlā) is eternal, and as for their forms and accidents, each one of them is preceded by another, without beginning. And this is the school of Aristotle and his followers among the early and later [thinkers].
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage describes the second possibility: the view that bodies are eternal in both essence and accidents. Al-Razi specifies that this is the position of some philosophers, particularly the Peripatetic school of Aristotle. He then details their view: celestial bodies (al-ajsām al-falakiyya) are eternal in essence and in their determinate attributes, except for their motions—each motion is caused by a prior motion ad infinitum. Elemental bodies (al-ajsām al-ʿunṣuriyya) have eternal matter (hayūlā), but their forms and accidents are temporally successive, each preceded by another without beginning. This is the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers.

Key Terms
قديمة (qadīma, eternal)
الأجسام الفلكية (al-ajsām al-falakiyya, celestial bodies)
الأجسام العنصرية (al-ajsām al-ʿunṣuriyya, elemental bodies)
هيولى (hayūlā, matter)
صور (ṣuwar, forms)
أعراض (aʿrāḍ, accidents)
أرسطاطاليس (Aristotle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This view denies all change in the world.
Correction: It actually affirms perpetual change for elemental bodies (forms and accidents) and celestial motions; only the underlying essences are eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the Aristotelian view as described, which bodies have eternal matter?
A) Celestial bodies
B) Elemental bodies
C) Both celestial and elemental
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Aristotelian view posits a universe that is eternal in its basic components but characterized by perpetual change in celestial motions and elemental forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe were eternal as Aristotle claimed, with no beginning and no Creator, what would ground your sense of purpose and accountability? Does the Islamic teaching of creation ex nihilo change how you view your own actions as matters of ultimate significance?
?
Imagine that every motion and form is just one in an endless chain with no first cause. How would that affect your trust that there is a final meaning to your efforts? Right now, are you living as if your actions are part of a purposeful story with a divine beginning and end?
?
Al-Razi presents this view as a serious possibility, not a straw man. Have you ever honestly considered why you believe the universe began? What would be at stake in your daily life if you truly doubted that Allah is the First Cause of all existence?

First Faction: Bodies as Eternal with Diverse Cosmogonies
Al-Razi enumerates the first faction's various cosmogonies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first faction's view that eternal selves were bodies and the various ancient cosmogonies associated with it.

Core Idea
Eternal bodies with diverse origins: earth, fire, air, water, vapor, or atoms.

Al-Razi continues his survey of possible positions on bodies. The third possibility—bodies eternal in essence, created in accidents—was held by most pre-Aristotelian philosophers. He divides them into two factions. This lesson covers the first faction, which affirms that the eternal selves themselves were bodies. Their views range from a single primordial element to atomic particles colliding to form the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
This passage lists several origin theories.
2
Identify the different origin theories
Look for each theory: earth, fire, air, vapor, water, and atomic particles. Note how each explains the generation of other elements.
3
Examine the atomic theory in detail
The longest description is of spherical solid particles in void. Understand how motion, collision, and rotation produce the heavens, elements, and compounds.
This resembles ancient Greek atomism.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why Al-Razi includes so many views. His purpose is to catalog the logical possibilities, not to endorse them.
This is a doxographic exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثالث: وهو أن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها. فهذا قول أكثر الفلاسفة الذين كانوا قبل (أرسطاطاليس) ثم هؤلاء فريقان : الفريق الأول : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة كانت أجساما . وهؤلاء منهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو التراب. وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنه بالتلطف. ومنهم من عكس وقال : أصل الأشياء هو النار ، وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنها بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال أصل الأشياء هو الهواء. وكون العنصر اللطيف عنه: بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو البخار، وكون العنصرين اللطيفين عنه : بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو الماء. ثم إن الماء تحرك، فأوجبت حركته سخونة، فتصاعد على وجه الماء زبد وارتفع منه دخان فتكونت الأرض من ذلك الزبد والسماء من ذلك الدخان. ومنهم من قال : أصل العالم أجزاء جسمانية كرية صلبة. ولما كانت أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة، لم يكن بقاء كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء في حيز معين من ذلك أولى من حصوله في حيز آخر، فلا جرم وجب فيها أن تكون متحركة من الأزل إلى الأبد. ثم اتفق لتلك الأجزاء أن تصادمت على شكل خاص، فتمانعت بسبب حركاتها المتدافعة فتكونت السماوات بهذا الطريق ثم إنها لما استدارت وكان باطنها مملوءًا من الأجسام عرض لما كان في غاية القرب من السموات، أن تسخن جدا وهو النار، وعرض لما كان في غاية البعد من السموات، أن تكاثف وبرد جدا وهو الأرض، والذي كان قريبا من النار، هو الهواء. والذي كان أبعد منه الماء. لأن الهواء ألطف وأسخن من الماء، ثم اختلطت هذه العناصر الأربعة، بسبب حركات الأجرام الفلكية، وتولدت المركبات من المعادن والنبات والحيوان. والفريق الثاني : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة ما كانت أجساما فهؤلاء ثلاث طوائف.
الطائفة الأولى : الذين قالوا : الأجسام مركبة من الهيولى والصورة. فالهيولى كانت قديمة، وكانت خالية عن الجسم، ثم حدثت الصورة الجسمية فيها، فحدثت الأجسام. والطائفة الثانية : الذين قالوا : العالم إنما تولد من امتزاج النور بالظلمة، وأما الأنوار والظلمات، فإنها قديمة. وهذا قول الثنوية. والطائفة الثالثة : الذين قالوا : أصل الأجسام الوحدات. وذلك لأن الوحدة إذا كانت مجردة عن الوضع والإشارة كانت مجرد وحدات . وإذا كانت الوحدة مشارًا إليها صارت نقطة. فإذا تركب نقطتان صار خطا . وإذا تركب خطان، صار سطحا. وإذا تركب سطحان صار جسما، فأصل الأجسام الوحدات وهي أمور قديمة قائمة بذواتها. فهذا شرح هذه الأقوال على الاختصار.
And as for the third possibility: that bodies are eternal in their essences, originated in their attributes. This is the statement of most of the philosophers who were before Aristotle. Then these are two groups:
The first group: those who said that the eternal selves were bodies. Among these: some said the origin of things is earth, and the remaining three elements come from it by attenuation. Some reversed and said the origin of things is fire, and the remaining three elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is air, and the subtle element comes from it by attenuation, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is vapor, and the two subtle elements come from it by attenuation, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is water. Then water moved, so its motion caused heat, so foam rose on the surface of the water and smoke rose from it, so the earth was formed from that foam and the sky from that smoke. Some said: the origin of the world is spherical solid corporeal particles. Since the parts of void were similar, the staying of each one of those parts in a particular locus was not more appropriate than its occurrence in another locus, so necessarily they had to be in motion from eternity to eternity. Then it happened that those particles collided in a particular shape, so they resisted each other due to their conflicting motions, and the heavens were formed in this way. Then when they rotated and their interior was filled with bodies, what was very near to the heavens became extremely hot—that is fire—and what was very far from the heavens became dense and very cold—that is earth. What was near the fire is air, and what was farther than it is water, because air is subtler and hotter than water. Then these four elements mixed due to the motions of celestial bodies, and compounds of minerals, plants, and animals were generated.
The second group: those who said that the eternal selves were not bodies. These are three sects.
The first sect: those who said that bodies are composed of matter (hayūlā) and form. So matter was eternal and free of body, then corporeal form occurred in it, so bodies occurred. The second sect: those who said that the world was generated only from the mixture of light and darkness. As for the lights and darknesses, they are eternal. This is the saying of the Dualists. The third sect: those who said that the origin of bodies is monads. That is because when the unit (waḥda) is stripped of position and indication, it is a mere unit. And when the unit is indicated, it becomes a point. If two points combine, they become a line. If two lines combine, they become a surface. If two surfaces combine, they become a body. So the origin of bodies is monads, and they are eternal things subsisting in themselves. So this is the explanation of these statements in brief.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the third possibility: bodies are eternal in their essences but originated in their attributes. Al-Razi attributes this view to most pre-Aristotelian philosophers. He divides them into two main factions.
The first faction maintains that the eternal selves (al-dhawāt al-qadīma) were bodies themselves. Within this faction, he enumerates various ancient cosmogonies: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Heraclitus (fire?), and others including earth, vapor, and a Democritean-like atomic theory with colliding particles forming the cosmos. The description includes a lengthy physical cosmology of how elements formed from rotation.
The second faction holds that the eternal selves were not bodies. This faction has three sub-sects: (1) Hylomorphists who say bodies are composed of eternal matter (hayūlā) and form, where corporeal form occurs in matter to produce bodies. (2) Dualists who say the world arises from mixture of eternal light and darkness. (3) Atomists (or monadists) who say bodies originate from indivisible units (waḥdāt) that, when posited, become points then lines, surfaces, and bodies. Al-Razi concludes by saying this is a brief explanation of these views.

Key Terms
قديمة بذواتها
محدثة بصفاتها
الفريق الأول
التراب
النار
الهواء
البخار
الماء
أجزاء جسمانية كرية صلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi endorses one of these cosmogonies.
Correction: Al-Razi is merely cataloguing the logical possibilities and historical views without endorsement.

Checkpoint
According to the first faction of the third possibility, what are the eternal selves?
A) Not bodies
B) Bodies
C) Both bodies and not bodies
D) Only monads

Key Takeaway
The first faction held that eternal selves were bodies, with multiple theories about their material origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading these ancient speculations about the origin of the universe, do you find that your own understanding comes from divine revelation or from human conjecture? Where do you place your trust when considering how the world began?
?
The philosophers attributed the formation of the heavens and earth to chance collisions of particles and eternal motion. Is there any area of your life where you act as if things happen by accident, forgetting that Allah has created everything with purpose and wisdom?
?
These thinkers had no access to prophethood or divine books. Yet you have the Qur’an and the Sunnah. Are you truly grateful for this guidance, or do you still rely on your own reasoning in matters of creed, falling into similar confusion?

Second Faction: Non-Bodily Eternal Selves and Three Sects
Al-Razi enumerates the three sects of the second faction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second faction's rejection of bodies as eternal selves and its three sub-sects: hylomorphism, dualism, and monadism.

Core Idea
Eternal selves are not bodies; they are matter, light/darkness, or monads.

Having presented the first faction's bodily views, Al-Razi now turns to the second faction of pre-Aristotelian philosophers. These thinkers maintained that the eternal selves were not bodies. Within this group, he identifies three distinct sects: those who posit eternal matter and form, the dualists who posit light and darkness, and the monadists who posit indivisible units.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second faction
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the division into three sects.
2
Identify the three sects
Look for the labels: الطائفة الأولى, الثانية, الثالثة. Each has a distinct theory.
They are hylomorphism, dualism, and monadism.
3
Understand each sect's claim
For each, note how they explain the origin of bodies from non-bodily foundations.
Compare their mechanisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثالث: وهو أن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها. فهذا قول أكثر الفلاسفة الذين كانوا قبل (أرسطاطاليس) ثم هؤلاء فريقان : الفريق الأول : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة كانت أجساما . وهؤلاء منهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو التراب. وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنه بالتلطف. ومنهم من عكس وقال : أصل الأشياء هو النار ، وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنها بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال أصل الأشياء هو الهواء. وكون العنصر اللطيف عنه: بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو البخار، وكون العنصرين اللطيفين عنه : بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو الماء. ثم إن الماء تحرك، فأوجبت حركته سخونة، فتصاعد على وجه الماء زبد وارتفع منه دخان فتكونت الأرض من ذلك الزبد والسماء من ذلك الدخان. ومنهم من قال : أصل العالم أجزاء جسمانية كرية صلبة. ولما كانت أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة، لم يكن بقاء كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء في حيز معين من ذلك أولى من حصوله في حيز آخر، فلا جرم وجب فيها أن تكون متحركة من الأزل إلى الأبد. ثم اتفق لتلك الأجزاء أن تصادمت على شكل خاص، فتمانعت بسبب حركاتها المتدافعة فتكونت السماوات بهذا الطريق ثم إنها لما استدارت وكان باطنها مملوءًا من الأجسام عرض لما كان في غاية القرب من السموات، أن تسخن جدا وهو النار، وعرض لما كان في غاية البعد من السموات، أن تكاثف وبرد جدا وهو الأرض، والذي كان قريبا من النار، هو الهواء. والذي كان أبعد منه الماء. لأن الهواء ألطف وأسخن من الماء، ثم اختلطت هذه العناصر الأربعة، بسبب حركات الأجرام الفلكية، وتولدت المركبات من المعادن والنبات والحيوان. والفريق الثاني : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة ما كانت أجساما فهؤلاء ثلاث طوائف.
الطائفة الأولى : الذين قالوا : الأجسام مركبة من الهيولى والصورة. فالهيولى كانت قديمة، وكانت خالية عن الجسم، ثم حدثت الصورة الجسمية فيها، فحدثت الأجسام. والطائفة الثانية : الذين قالوا : العالم إنما تولد من امتزاج النور بالظلمة، وأما الأنوار والظلمات، فإنها قديمة. وهذا قول الثنوية. والطائفة الثالثة : الذين قالوا : أصل الأجسام الوحدات. وذلك لأن الوحدة إذا كانت مجردة عن الوضع والإشارة كانت مجرد وحدات . وإذا كانت الوحدة مشارًا إليها صارت نقطة. فإذا تركب نقطتان صار خطا . وإذا تركب خطان، صار سطحا. وإذا تركب سطحان صار جسما، فأصل الأجسام الوحدات وهي أمور قديمة قائمة بذواتها. فهذا شرح هذه الأقوال على الاختصار.
And as for the third possibility: that bodies are eternal in their essences, originated in their attributes. This is the statement of most of the philosophers who were before Aristotle. Then these are two groups:
The first group: those who said that the eternal selves were bodies. Among these: some said the origin of things is earth, and the remaining three elements come from it by attenuation. Some reversed and said the origin of things is fire, and the remaining three elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is air, and the subtle element comes from it by attenuation, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is vapor, and the two subtle elements come from it by attenuation, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation. Some said the origin of things is water. Then water moved, so its motion caused heat, so foam rose on the surface of the water and smoke rose from it, so the earth was formed from that foam and the sky from that smoke. Some said: the origin of the world is spherical solid corporeal particles. Since the parts of void were similar, the staying of each one of those parts in a particular locus was not more appropriate than its occurrence in another locus, so necessarily they had to be in motion from eternity to eternity. Then it happened that those particles collided in a particular shape, so they resisted each other due to their conflicting motions, and the heavens were formed in this way. Then when they rotated and their interior was filled with bodies, what was very near to the heavens became extremely hot—that is fire—and what was very far from the heavens became dense and very cold—that is earth. What was near the fire is air, and what was farther than it is water, because air is subtler and hotter than water. Then these four elements mixed due to the motions of celestial bodies, and compounds of minerals, plants, and animals were generated.
The second group: those who said that the eternal selves were not bodies. These are three sects.
The first sect: those who said that bodies are composed of matter (hayūlā) and form. So matter was eternal and free of body, then corporeal form occurred in it, so bodies occurred. The second sect: those who said that the world was generated only from the mixture of light and darkness. As for the lights and darknesses, they are eternal. This is the saying of the Dualists. The third sect: those who said that the origin of bodies is monads. That is because when the unit (waḥda) is stripped of position and indication, it is a mere unit. And when the unit is indicated, it becomes a point. If two points combine, they become a line. If two lines combine, they become a surface. If two surfaces combine, they become a body. So the origin of bodies is monads, and they are eternal things subsisting in themselves. So this is the explanation of these statements in brief.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the third possibility: bodies are eternal in their essences but originated in their attributes. Al-Razi attributes this view to most pre-Aristotelian philosophers. He divides them into two main factions.
The first faction maintains that the eternal selves (al-dhawāt al-qadīma) were bodies themselves. Within this faction, he enumerates various ancient cosmogonies: Thales (water), Anaximenes (air), Heraclitus (fire?), and others including earth, vapor, and a Democritean-like atomic theory with colliding particles forming the cosmos. The description includes a lengthy physical cosmology of how elements formed from rotation.
The second faction holds that the eternal selves were not bodies. This faction has three sub-sects: (1) Hylomorphists who say bodies are composed of eternal matter (hayūlā) and form, where corporeal form occurs in matter to produce bodies. (2) Dualists who say the world arises from mixture of eternal light and darkness. (3) Atomists (or monadists) who say bodies originate from indivisible units (waḥdāt) that, when posited, become points then lines, surfaces, and bodies. Al-Razi concludes by saying this is a brief explanation of these views.

هيولى
صورة جسمية
الثنوية
الوحدات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Monadism is the same as Islamic atomism.
Correction: Islamic theology's atomism (jawhar fard) is distinct from these Greek monads, though similar in concept.

The second faction holds eternal selves are not bodies, with three distinct explanations of how bodies arise from non-bodily principles.

?
The monadists believe that simple units are the ultimate building blocks, eternal and self-subsisting. Where does your trust ultimately rest? Do you rely on created means or the Creator directly? Let this lesson push you to examine if any philosophical assumption has weakened your tawheed.
?
You just read that some say matter is eternal and forms happen later. Have you ever assumed that the material world has always existed alongside Allah? This is a hidden form of shirk. Ask yourself: do I truly believe that everything besides Allah is created from nothing? If not, what must you change today?

The Fourth and Fifth Possibilities: Eternal Attributes with Created Essence and Suspension of Judgment
Al-Razi presents the fourth and fifth possibilities: eternal attributes with created essence (rejected as self-evidently false) and suspension of judgment (attributed to Galen).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth and fifth possibilities in Al-Razi's classification of positions on the world's eternity, and why one is self-evidently false while the other is attributed to Galen.

Core Idea
Some logical possibilities are so obviously false they need no argument; others are held by historical figures like Galen.

Al-Razi continues his survey of possible positions on whether the world is eternal or created. Not all possibilities are actually held by real thinkers; some are so self-evidently absurd that no one has ever defended them. Others, like suspension of judgment, have been adopted by specific philosophers. In this lesson, we examine the fourth and fifth possibilities from his list.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that there are two distinct possibilities: the fourth and the fifth.
The fourth possibility is about eternal attributes with a created essence; the fifth is about suspending judgment.
2
Notice the difference in treatment
Observe that Al-Razi handles the fourth and fifth possibilities very differently. The fourth is declared false by self-evidence (badaha) and he notes no one has accepted it. The fifth is simply attributed to Galen without further comment.
3
Reflect on logical completeness
Ask yourself why Al-Razi includes a position he considers self-evidently false. This is part of his method of exhaustive enumeration—listing all logical possibilities even if some are absurd.
This connects to the broader structure of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الرابع: بأن يقال : العالم قديم الصفات محدث الذات. وهذا معلوم البطلان بالبداهة فلا جرم لم يقبل به قائل
الاحتمال الخامس : التوقف في هذه الأقسام وعدم القطع بواحد منها. وهو قول (جالينوس).
The fourth possibility: that it be said: the world is eternal in attributes, originated in essence. And this is known to be false by self-evidence, so no speaker has accepted it.
The fifth possibility: suspension of judgment concerning these categories and not deciding on any one of them. And this is the saying of Galen.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth possibilities concisely. The fourth possibility—that the world is eternal in its attributes but originated in its essence—is declared self-evidently false (bāṭil bi al-badāha), and Al-Razi notes that no proponent has ever accepted it. The fifth possibility is suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) regarding all the categories, attributed to Galen (Jālīnūs). This completes the survey of the five possible positions.

البطلان بالبداهة (al-buṭlān bi al-badāha, false by self-evidence)
توقف (tawaqquf, suspension of judgment)
جالينوس (Galen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each of Al-Razi's five possibilities corresponds to a known school of thought.
Correction: The fourth possibility—eternal attributes with created essence—is not attributed to any actual group; al-Razi includes it for logical completeness.
