Proof from Necessary-by-Other and Composition
Second proof: necessary by itself and necessary by other lead to composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between 'necessary by itself' and 'necessary by another' leads to composition in the essence of the Necessary Being, implying multiplicity.

Core Idea
Even if only one being is necessary by itself, necessary-by-others exist, and their shared necessity with it forces composition.

The second argument from Razi's third objection assumes for the sake of argument that there is only one necessary-by-itself. It then introduces the category of 'necessary by another'—things that become necessary when their cause exists. The argument shows that the necessary-by-itself shares the attribute of necessity with these others, but is distinguished by being necessary in itself. This leads to composition in its essence, which was earlier argued to be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the hypothetical assumption and the reasoning that follows.
The phrase 'هب أن' indicates a supposition.
2
Trace the argument's logical steps
Identify the claim: the necessary-by-itself shares 'being necessary' with necessary-by-others, but differs by being necessary in itself. The basis of sharing and distinguishing must be different, hence composition.
This mirrors the classic argument that if two things share a property and differ by another, they are composite.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that if the necessary-by-itself is composite, then it cannot be unitary, and the impossibility of composition (as argued earlier) would apply.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أنه يلزم من حصول الاشتراك في الوجوب وحصول التباين في التعين وقوع الكثرة في الماهية لكن هذه الكثرة لازمة، سواء قلنا بأن واجب الوجود واحدًا أو لم نقل به، وبيانه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه لا شك أن هنا لوازم وملزومات فإن الثلاثة مستلزمة لذاتها لمعنى الفردية والأربعة مستلزمة لمعنى الزوجية والسواد لذاته مناف للبياض، فهذه الاستلزامات الذاتية وجوبات ذاتية وهي أنواع كثيرة فإن واجب الوجود لذاته أكثر من واحد. وثانيها : هب أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا واحداً، لكن الواجب بغيره فيه كثرة، ثم الواجب لذاته يشارك الواجب بغيره في مسمى كونه واجباً، ويمتاز عنه بخصوص كونه واجبًا لذاته وما به المشاركة مغاير لما به المباينة، فالواجب بالذات مركب في ماهيته وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة. وثالثها: أن واجب الوجود لذاته يساوي في كونه موجودا سائر الموجودات ويباينها بتعينه وذلك التعين إما أن يكون سلبيًا أو ثبوتياً، فإن كان ذلك التعين سلبيًا يكون هو من حيث إنه هو عدما لا وجوداً، وذلك محال، فإذن ذلك التعين الذي به باين غيره أمر ثبوتي، فإذن هو مركب عن الوجود الذي به يشارك غيره، وعن التعين الذي به امتاز عن غيره، فيلزم وقوع الكثرة في ذاته.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that even the Necessary Being cannot share a universal concept without forcing composition. Now look at your own life: How many aspects of your identity—your status, knowledge, existence—are actually borrowed from others? Can you point to one thing that is truly yours by yourself? If nothing, what does that mean for your self-reliance before Allah today? Will you let go of one false sense of independence today and openly acknowledge your complete dependence on Him?
?
The proof shows that the Necessary Being is unique and incomposite. Contrast that with your own heart, which is divided by multiple attachments and loyalties. How many 'necessities' do you serve besides Allah? What concrete step will you take after this lesson to simplify your devotion to the One who alone is necessary by Himself?
?
The passage uses the concepts of sharing and distinction to argue for composition. Reflect on your prayer: when you recite 'Iyyaka na'budu wa iyyaka nasta'in', you affirm that worship and aid come only from Allah. Does your heart truly make that distinction, or do you share your reliance among other things? What specific action will you change today to ensure your worship is purely for Him?

Proof from Determination (Ta'ayyun)
Third proof: determination as positive attribute forces composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary being's determination (متعين) must be a positive attribute, leading to composition of existence and determination.

Core Idea
If determination is negative, the being would be non-existence; so it's positive, causing composition.

The third proof focuses on the concept of determination (تعين) that distinguishes the Necessary Being from other existents. The argument explores whether this determination is negative (i.e., just the absence of other attributes) or positive (a real attribute). If negative, then the being would in that aspect be mere non-existence, which is absurd. Therefore, determination must be positive, leading to composition of existence and determination, and thus multiplicity in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the text carefully, especially the dichotomy between negative and positive determination.
The argument is a classic dilemma: either the distinction is negative or positive.
2
Work through the dichotomy
Understand why negative determination is impossible: if the Necessary Being is distinct only by a negation, then in that respect it would be non-existence.
This assumes that a real distinction cannot be based on pure negation.
3
Conclude the argument
Since determination must be positive, the Necessary Being is composed of existence (shared with others) and determination (unique), thus multiplicity in essence follows.
This is another angle to show composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : سلمنا أنه يلزم من حصول الاشتراك في الوجوب وحصول التباين في التعين وقوع الكثرة في الماهية لكن هذه الكثرة لازمة، سواء قلنا بأن واجب الوجود واحدًا أو لم نقل به، وبيانه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه لا شك أن هنا لوازم وملزومات فإن الثلاثة مستلزمة لذاتها لمعنى الفردية والأربعة مستلزمة لمعنى الزوجية والسواد لذاته مناف للبياض، فهذه الاستلزامات الذاتية وجوبات ذاتية وهي أنواع كثيرة فإن واجب الوجود لذاته أكثر من واحد. وثانيها : هب أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا واحداً، لكن الواجب بغيره فيه كثرة، ثم الواجب لذاته يشارك الواجب بغيره في مسمى كونه واجباً، ويمتاز عنه بخصوص كونه واجبًا لذاته وما به المشاركة مغاير لما به المباينة، فالواجب بالذات مركب في ماهيته وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة. وثالثها: أن واجب الوجود لذاته يساوي في كونه موجودا سائر الموجودات ويباينها بتعينه وذلك التعين إما أن يكون سلبيًا أو ثبوتياً، فإن كان ذلك التعين سلبيًا يكون هو من حيث إنه هو عدما لا وجوداً، وذلك محال، فإذن ذلك التعين الذي به باين غيره أمر ثبوتي، فإذن هو مركب عن الوجود الذي به يشارك غيره، وعن التعين الذي به امتاز عن غيره، فيلزم وقوع الكثرة في ذاته.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fourth Objection: Composition and Contingency
Fourth objection and beginning of response about composition and contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against proofs of God's unity, which argues that composition of necessary parts is possible, and the beginning of the response that every possible being requires an effecting cause.

Core Idea
Can the Necessary Being be composed of multiple necessary parts?

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised against arguments for God's unity. The objector concedes that multiplicity might follow from previous proofs but argues that each part could be necessary by itself, making the whole necessary without contradiction. The objection then shifts to challenge the claim that every possible being must be originated. We see the start of the response: the first premise that everything possible needs an effecting cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once. Notice the two parts of the objection: first about composition, then about contingency and origination.
The response is incomplete; the text cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Understand the composition argument
The objector says that even if multiplicity follows from previous arguments, each part of the essence could be necessary by itself, making the whole necessary without any problem.
3
Analyze the contingency challenge
The objector then concedes that everything other than the One is possible, but questions why every possible being must be originated (hadith).
4
Identify the response's first premise
The response begins by stating the first premise: everything possible by itself is in need of an effecting cause. The text cuts off before completing the argument.
Consider what the next premise or conclusion might be.

The response begins by stating the first premise: everything possible needs an effecting cause, and everything needing a cause is originated. The text cuts off as it begins to discuss that premise.

Third Objection: Multiplicity is Necessary
Opponent argues that multiplicity in essence is necessary and unavoidable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent argues that multiplicity in the divine essence is necessary and unavoidable, and you will analyze the three proofs presented.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in essence is inevitable, even if there is only one Necessary Existent.

This passage presents the third objection against divine unity. The opponent concedes that if two necessary beings share necessity but differ in determination, multiplicity would follow. However, he boldly claims that such multiplicity is necessary regardless of whether one affirms one or more necessary beings. He then provides three distinct proofs to support this claim. Let's explore his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the complete passage once to get the flow of the objection. Notice the concessive tone ('سلمنا') and the three numbered arguments.
2
Identify the main claim
The opponent states that multiplicity is necessary whether we affirm one necessary being or not. Underline this sentence in the translation.
This is the core idea.
3
Examine the three proofs
Go through each of the three proofs: (1) essential necessities, (2) sharing and differencing, (3) determination as positive. Notice how each leads to multiplicity.
The proofs are numbered in the text.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this objection challenges the theologian's position. What would be the consequences if this argument stands?
This connects to the broader debate on tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: سلمنا أنه يلزم من حصول الاشتراك في الوجوب وحصول التباين في التعين وقوع الكثرة في الماهية لكن هذه الكثرة لازمة، سواء قلنا بأن واجب الوجود واحدًا أو لم نقل به، وبيانه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه لا شك أن هنا لوازم وملزومات فإن الثلاثة مستلزمة لذاتها لمعنى الفردية والأربعة مستلزمة لمعنى الزوجية والسواد لذاته مناف للبياض، فهذه الاستلزامات الذاتية وجوبات ذاتية وهي أنواع كثيرة فإن واجب الوجود لذاته أكثر من واحد. وثانيها : هب أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا واحداً، لكن الواجب بغيره فيه كثرة، ثم الواجب لذاته يشارك الواجب بغيره في مسمى كونه واجباً، ويمتاز عنه بخصوص كونه واجبًا لذاته وما به المشاركة مغاير لما به المباينة، فالواجب بالذات مركب في ماهيته وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة. وثالثها: أن واجب الوجود لذاته يساوي في كونه موجودا سائر الموجودات ويباينها بتعينه وذلك التعين إما أن يكون سلبيًا أو ثبوتياً، فإن كان ذلك التعين سلبيًا يكون هو من حيث إنه هو عدما لا وجوداً، وذلك محال، فإذن ذلك التعين الذي به باين غيره أمر ثبوتي، فإذن هو مركب عن الوجود الذي به يشارك غيره، وعن التعين الذي به امتاز عن غيره، فيلزم وقوع الكثرة في ذاته.
The third question: We concede that from the occurrence of sharing in necessity (wujub) and the occurrence of differentiation in determination (ta'ayyun) results multiplicity in the essence, but this multiplicity is necessary, whether we say that the Necessary Existent is one or not, and its explanation is from several aspects: First: There is no doubt that there are necessary concomitants and that which they necessitate. For example, three necessitates by its very essence the meaning of oddness, four necessitates the meaning of evenness, and blackness by its essence is incompatible with whiteness. These essential necessitations are essential necessities and they are many types, so the Necessary Existent by essence would be more than one. Second: Suppose that the Necessary Existent by essence is only one, but the necessary by another (al-wajib bi-ghayrihi) involves multiplicity. Then the necessary by essence shares with the necessary by another in the name of being necessary, and differs from it by the specificity of being necessary by essence. That by which it shares is different from that by which it differs, so the necessary by essence is composite in its essence, and then the aforementioned impossibilities return. Third: The Necessary Existent by essence equals other existents in being existent and differs from them by its determination. That determination is either negative or positive. If it is negative, then it would be non-being not existence, which is impossible. Thus the determination by which it differs from others is something positive, so it is composed of existence by which it shares with others and the determination by which it is distinguished from others, so multiplicity in its essence follows.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the claim that God is one. The opponent concedes that if two necessary beings share in necessity (wujub) and differ in determination (ta'ayyun), there would be multiplicity in the essence. However, he argues that this multiplicity is necessary and unavoidable. He gives three proofs: First, there are many essential necessities (wajubat dhatiyya) like the oddness of three and the evenness of four, which imply multiple wajibs. Second, even if only one Necessary by essence exists, the necessary by another (e.g., the contingent) involves multiplicity, and since the necessary by essence shares the name 'necessary' with the necessary by another but differs by being necessary by essence, composition follows. Third, the Necessary by essence shares existence with others but differs by determination; if that determination were negative, it would be non-existent, which is impossible, so it must be positive, leading to composition of existence and determination. Thus the objection shows that the opponent thinks multiplicity is inevitable, whether one affirms one necessary being or multiple.

Key Terms
وجوب
تعين
واجب الوجود
ماهية
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent is asserting that there are multiple necessary beings.
Correction: The opponent is not asserting multiplicity; he is arguing that multiplicity is a necessary consequence whether one affirms one or many.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the third objection?
A) That multiplicity in the essence is impossible
B) That multiplicity is necessary whether one affirms one or many
C) That the Necessary Existent must be composite
D) That determination is negative

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that multiplicity in the essence is necessary, using three proofs that challenge the simplicity of the Necessary Existent.

The Fourth Objection: Composition from Necessary Parts
Opponent questions why composition of necessary parts is problematic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's objection that a composite of necessary-by-essence parts could be necessary without difficulty.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that a whole composed of independently necessary parts is still necessary.

The text presents the fourth objection to the argument that composition implies need. The opponent grants that multiplicity occurs, but questions why it is problematic. He proposes that each part could be necessary by itself, and the composite necessary by virtue of its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text aloud or silently, then follow the literal translation to grasp the exact wording of the objection.
Notice the phrase 'وجزء كل واحد منهما واجب لذاته' — 'each one of the two parts is necessary by itself.'
2
Identify the opponent's key claim
The opponent asserts that each part is necessary by itself (wajib li-dhatihi), so the whole composed of them is necessary. This avoids the problem of needing an external cause.
3
Consider why this might be problematic
Think about the implications: if each part is necessary by itself, then there would be multiple necessary beings. How might that conflict with divine simplicity?
This connects to upcoming lessons on the author's refutation.
4
Reflect on the subtlety
The objection shifts the burden: it challenges the assumption that composition necessarily implies need. Reflect on whether this solution is coherent.
The common misunderstanding is that the objection does not explain how multiple necessary parts can coexist without external dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: هب أنه يلزم من ذلك وقوع الكثرة في ذات كل واحد منهما، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال كل واحد من جزئي ذاته واجب لذاته، وذلك المجموع يجب لوجوب جزأيه معا وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم منه محذور أصلا؟
The fourth question: Suppose that from that follows multiplicity in the essence of each of them, but why may it not be said that each one of the two parts of its essence is necessary by itself, and that composite is necessary due to the necessity of its two parts together, and on this assumption no difficulty follows at all?
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth objection. The opponent grants that multiplicity would occur in the essence of each supposed necessary being. However, he questions why it should be problematic if we say that each part of the essence is itself necessary by essence (wajib li-dhatihi), and the composite whole becomes necessary by virtue of the necessity of its parts. The opponent claims that on this assumption, no logical difficulty arises. This objection attempts to avoid the problem of composition by asserting that the parts are independent necessary beings, so the whole is a sum of necessary parts rather than a composite requiring an external cause.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
جزء
مجموع
محذور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's solution is logically sound and resolves the composition problem without difficulty.
Correction: The objection does not explain how multiple necessary-by-essence parts can coexist without external dependency or contradiction to divine simplicity.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent propose as a solution to the composition problem?
A) That the parts are contingent
B) That each part is necessary by itself and the whole is necessary by its parts
C) That determination is negative
D) That the essence has no parts

Key Takeaway
The opponent attempts to avoid the problem of composition by asserting that the whole is necessary because its parts are each necessary by themselves.

Al-Razi's Responses to Objections on the Oneness of the Necessary Being
Al-Razi answers three objections about the multiplicity of necessary beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes three key objections against the oneness of the Necessary Being by clarifying the meaning of 'wajib li-dhatihi' and distinguishing between essence and existence.

Core Idea
Shared attributes don't imply multiple necessary beings; true necessity is self-sufficiency.

In this passage, Al-Razi engages with three objections that challenge the Islamic doctrine of tawhid concerning the Necessary Being. Each objection attempts to show multiplicity by appealing to shared attributes like necessity or existence. Al-Razi systematically responds by refining the definition of 'necessary by itself' and distinguishing between essence and existence, though he acknowledges a lingering difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to get an overview of the three objections and responses. Notice the pattern: 'قوله' introduces an objection, 'قلنا' introduces the response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection argues that necessary attributes (like three being odd) imply multiple necessary beings. Focus on Al-Razi's response: he distinguishes between 'necessary by itself' and accidental necessary attributes.
Note the definition of 'wajib li-dhatihi' as independent and self-initiating.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection points out that the Necessary Being shares necessity with contingent necessary beings. See how Al-Razi uses a 'negative qualification' (قيد سلبي) to distinguish them, preventing multiplicity.
4
Study the third objection and the unresolved issue
The third objection claims that sharing existence with possibles leads to multiplicity. Al-Razi distinguishes existence from essence but then raises a problem: if essence causes existence eternally, it implies effect without temporal origination. He leaves this for divine guidance.
This shows the depth of rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "كون الثلاثة فردًا والأربعة زوجًا أمور واجبة لذواتها، فإذن واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد" قلنا مرادنا من قولنا واجب الوجود لذاته الموجود الذي يكون مستقلاً ومستبدأ بذاته وتحققه وهذه صفات تكون لاحقة للماهيات فلا تكون واجبة لذواتها.
قوله : "الواجب لذاته يشارك الواجب لغيره في مسمى الوجوب" قلنا : لكنه يمتاز عنه بقيد سلبي، وعلى هذا التقدير لا تلزم الكثرة.
قوله : "الواجب لذاته يشارك الممكن لذاته في مسمى الموجودية ويخالفه في التعين فتلزم الكثرة" قلنا : مذهبنا أن الوجود الذي به المشاركة مغاير للماهية المخصوصة التي بها المخالفة لكن لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الماهية مستلزمة لذلك الوجود؟ إلا أنه يبقى ههنا إشكال وهو أنكم لما جعلتم تلك الماهية علة لذلك الوجود، مع كون الماهية والوجود دائمين فقد اعترفتم بأن استناد الأثر إلى المؤثر لا يتوقف على الحدوث، وهذا الإشكال مما نستخير الله تعالى فيه.
He said: "The fact that three is odd and four is even are things necessary by themselves, so the Necessary Being is more than one." We said: What we mean by "Necessary Being by virtue of itself" is the existent that is independent and initiated by itself and its actualization. These attributes are accidents of essences, so they are not necessary by themselves.
He said: "The Necessary Being by virtue of itself shares with the necessary by virtue of another in the meaning of necessity." We said: But it is distinguished from it by a negative qualification, and on this basis multiplicity does not arise.
He said: "The Necessary Being by virtue of itself shares with the possible by virtue of itself in the meaning of existence and differs in determination, so multiplicity arises." We said: Our doctrine is that the existence by which there is participation is other than the specific essence by which there is difference. But why is it not possible that that essence necessitates that existence? Except that there remains here an objection: that you have made that essence a cause for that existence, while the essence and existence are eternal, so you have acknowledged that the dependence of the effect on the cause does not require temporal origination. This objection is one in which we seek divine guidance.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the oneness of the Necessary Being and the author's responses. The first objection cites necessary attributes like odd and even being necessary by themselves, implying multiple necessary beings. The response clarifies that 'necessary by itself' means independent and self-initiating, not accidental attributes. The second objection points out that the Necessary Being shares necessity with contingent necessary beings. The response notes that they differ by a negative qualification, so no multiplicity follows. The third objection highlights that the Necessary Being shares existence with possibles but differs in determination. The response distinguishes existence from essence but raises an unresolved problem: if a specific essence necessitates existence eternally, then the effect depends on the cause without temporal origination, which the author leaves for divine guidance.

Key Terms
wajib al-wujud
al-wajib bi-ghayrihi
al-mumkin
al-mawjudiyya
al-qayd al-salbi
al-huduth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If multiple things share an attribute like necessity or existence, there must be multiple necessary beings.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the meaning of 'necessary by itself' is unique to the Necessary Being because it is independent and self-initiating. Shared attributes do not entail multiplicity when the distinction is based on causation and essence-existence relation.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, what does 'wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi' mean?
A) Any necessary attribute
B) The existent that is independent and self-initiating
C) The one that shares existence with others
D) The composite being

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's responses show that true oneness of the Necessary Being is preserved by understanding that shared attributes do not necessitate multiplicity when meanings are properly distinguished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to examine your own life, what 'necessary' things do you rely on that are actually dependent on others? How does this lesson challenge your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Al-Razi honestly admits an unresolved problem and seeks divine guidance. Are you willing to leave certain profound questions unanswered, trusting in God rather than forcing a false certainty?
?
The Necessary Being is truly independent. In what areas of your life are you still trying to be self-sufficient, refusing to acknowledge your total dependence on God? What concrete step can you take today to surrender that illusion?

8 min
Why Composite Necessary Beings Are Impossible
Al-Razi gives two proofs against assuming composite necessary beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs that a necessary being cannot be composite.

Core Idea
Every composite depends on its parts, so a necessary being cannot be composite.

Al-Razi refutes a sophisticated objection that necessary beings could be composed of a shared necessary existence and a differentiating determination. He offers two arguments that cut to the core: composition entails possibility, and composition leads to an infinite regress. Let's examine these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: someone proposes that two necessary beings could share a common existence and differ by a particular determination, each part being necessary by itself, making the composite necessary.
This is the hypothesis Al-Razi is about to refute.
2
First proof: composition entails possibility
If there were two necessary beings, each would be composite. Every composite is possible (since it depends on its parts). But a necessary being cannot be possible. Contradiction.
This proof relies on the principle that what depends on parts is contingent.
3
Second proof: infinite regress
If the parts themselves are necessary, they share necessity but differ in particularity. Then each part would itself be composite (sharing and differentiating), leading to infinite parts.
This shows that assuming composite necessary parts leads to an absurd infinite chain.
4
Conclusion
Both proofs demonstrate that composition is impossible for a necessary being. The objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "لم لا يجوز أن كل واحد من الموجودين الواجبين يكون مركبا من الوجود الذي به المشاركة، ومن التعين الذي به المباينة إلا أن كل واحد من هذين الجزأين يكون واجبًا لذاته، وحينئذٍ يكون المركب واجبًا لوجوب كل واحد من جزأيه؟ " قلنا : هذا باطل من وجهين :
الأول: أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبًا لذاته لزم كون كل واحد منهما مركباً، وكل مركب ممكن فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته غير واجب لذاته، وذلك محال، وإذا لزم المحال من هذا القدر كفانا هذا القدر في تقرير المطلوب.
الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا لو فرضنا كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين واجبًا فحينئذ يشركان في الوجوب ويتباينان في الخصوصية فيلزم كون كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين مركبًا من أجزاء لا نهاية لها.
He said: "Why is it not permissible that each of the two necessary existents be composed of the existence through which there is participation and the determination through which there is differentiation, such that each of these two parts is necessary by itself, and thus the composite is necessary due to the necessity of each of its parts?" We said: This is invalid in two ways:
First: If we assume two existents each of which is necessary by itself, then each would be composite, and every composite is possible, so the necessary by itself would be not necessary by itself, which is impossible. If impossibility follows from this much, this much suffices to establish the required conclusion.
Second: If we assume each of those two parts to be necessary, then they share in necessity and differ in particularity, so each of those two parts would be composed of infinite parts.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that one could conceive of two necessary beings each composed of a shared existence and a differentiating determination, making each part necessary by itself and the whole necessary through its parts. The author gives two refutations. First, if there were two necessary beings, each would be composite, and every composite is possible (since it depends on its parts), so a necessary being would be possible, a contradiction. Second, if the parts themselves were necessary, they would share necessity and differ in particularity, leading to each part being composed of infinite parts (since the same reasoning would apply recursively), which is impossible. These two arguments show that composition cannot occur in a necessary being.

Key Terms
al-tarkib
al-mumkin
al-wajib
ajza' la nihaya laha

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition is acceptable for necessary beings because the parts could be necessary themselves.
Correction: The text proves that composition entails possibility (dependency on parts) and leads to infinite regress, both incompatible with necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does the first refutation say a composite necessary being is impossible?
A) Because every composite is possible and a necessary being cannot be possible
B) Because there would be infinite parts
C) Because it would require a cause
D) Because it would share necessity

Key Takeaway
A necessary being cannot be composite because composition entails possibility and leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on countless things—your body, your mind, your relationships—to function. If a necessary being cannot be composite, what does that say about your own need to be grounded in something utterly simple and self-sufficient? Are you actually relying on that One, or are you living as though you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly internalized your absolute dependence on Him?
?
Al-Razi argues that composition leads to infinite regress and impossibility. Think of the chain of 'necessities' you construct in your life: 'I need this job to have money, I need money to live, I need to live to be happy...' Where does that chain end? Does it end in the Necessary Being, or does it trail off into infinity? What would it take to cut that chain and anchor your ultimate hope in the One who is not composed of parts?
?
The objection Al-Razi refutes assumed that a necessary being could be made of necessary parts. Are there things in your life you treat as 'necessary' that are actually composite and dependent—like your reputation, your security, or your identity? How does this passage expose the foolishness of treating any created thing as a necessary component of your well-being? Will you commit today to identifying one such false necessity and loosening your grip on it?

Refutation of Composition Argument for Multiple Necessary Beings
Razi refutes the claim that two necessary beings could be composed of necessary parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that a necessary being cannot be composite, and how this refutes the possibility of multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
If two necessary beings existed, they would be composite, and composition implies contingency.

In this passage, Razi responds to a hypothetical objection: could two necessary beings exist if each is composed of necessary parts? The objection tries to salvage multiple necessary beings by making them composite. Razi shows this leads to contradictions. Let's read his two-part refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the structure: objection, then two aspects.
Pay attention to the logical flow from objection to refutation.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument shows that if there are two necessary beings, each would be composite, but every composite is contingent, so necessary beings would be contingent, contradiction.
This is a straightforward reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument shows that if each part is necessary, they share necessity and differ in particularity, leading to infinite regress of parts.
This argument relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the implications
Recognize that this argument strengthens tawhid by showing logically that multiple necessary beings are impossible.
Consider how this underpins Islamic monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "لم لا يجوز أن كل واحد من الموجودين الواجبين يكون مركبا من الوجود الذي به المشاركة، ومن التعين الذي به المباينة إلا أن كل واحد من هذين الجزأين يكون واجبًا لذاته، وحينئذٍ يكون المركب واجبًا لوجوب كل واحد من جزأيه؟ " قلنا : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول: هو أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبًا لذاته لزم كون كل واحد منهما مركباً، وكل مركب ممكن فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته غير واجب لذاته، وذلك محال، وإذا لزم المحال من هذا القدر كفانا هذا القدر في تقرير المطلوب. الوجه الثاني : وهو أنا لو فرضنا كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين واجبًا فحينئذ يشركان في الوجوب ويتباينان في الخصوصية فيلزم كون كل واحد من ذينك الجزأين مركبًا من أجزاء لا نهاية لها.
His statement: 'Why is it not permissible that each of the two necessary existents be composed of the existence by which participation occurs, and of the determination by which distinction occurs, except that each of these two parts be necessary in itself, and then the composite would be necessary due to the necessity of each of its parts?' We said: This is false from two aspects: The first: If we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, then each of them would be composite, and every composite is contingent, so it would follow that the necessary in itself is not necessary in itself, and that is impossible. And if impossibility follows from this consideration, it suffices us in establishing the desired conclusion. The second aspect: If we suppose each of those two parts to be necessary, then they share in necessity and differ in particularity, so it follows that each of those two parts is composed of parts ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an objection and a two-part refutation. The objector suggests that two necessary beings could be composite, with each part being necessary in itself, thus the composite would be necessary. Razi responds with two arguments. First, if there were two necessary beings, each would be composite, but every composite is contingent (because it depends on its parts), so a necessary being would be contingent, which is a contradiction. This alone suffices to refute the objection. Second, if each part were necessary, they would share necessity and differ in particularity, so each part would itself be composite of a shared and a distinctive part, leading to an infinite regress of parts, which is absurd.

Key Terms
مركب
ممكن
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary being could be composite if its parts are necessary.
Correction: Razi shows that composition implies dependence (contingency), and also leads to infinite regress, so a necessary being cannot be composite.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the idea that a necessary being could be composed of necessary parts?
A) Because composition implies contingency.
B) Because parts cannot be necessary.
C) Because there can only be one necessary being.
D) Because the objector's argument is faulty for logical reasons.

Key Takeaway
A necessary being cannot be composite because composition entails contingency and leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat any person, position, or possession as if it were 'necessary' for your well-being—as if it could not be taken away? How does Razi's argument that only Allah is truly necessary challenge that attitude?
?
Consider a time you worried that losing something would 'undo' you. Did that thing become, in your heart, like a necessary being? What would it look like to truly believe that only Allah is necessary and everything else is contingent?
?
Razi shows that the very idea of two necessary beings leads to contradiction. Are there 'two masters' (e.g., Allah and wealth, Allah and reputation) you try to serve? What step can you take this week to uproot that contradiction in your own soul?

Fifth Proof: First Division (Eternity Identical to Bodyhood)
Razi rejects the identity of eternity and bodyhood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternity of a body cannot be identical to its being a body.

Core Idea
Knowledge of bodyhood does not entail knowledge of eternity.

Razi presents a proof by contradiction, examining two possibilities. The first is that eternity is the same as being a body. This lesson unpacks that claim and shows its absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the translation. Notice Razi's claim that two possibilities must be examined.
The passage is part of a longer proof.
2
Identify the two divisions
Razi first states that if the body were eternal, its eternity would either be identical to its being a body or additional.
He will then reject both.
3
Understand the rejection of the first division
Focus on why identity fails: because knowledge of bodyhood does not necessarily give knowledge of eternity.
This is a logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس في حدوث الأجسام : لو كان الجسم قديما لكان قدمه إما أن يكون عين كونه جسما وإما أن يكون مغايرا لكونه جسما، والقسمان باطلان، فيبطل القول بكون الجسم قديما إنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قدم الجسم عين كونه جسما لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان العلم يكونه جسما علما بكونه قديماً، فكما أن العلم بكونه جسمًا ضروريًا لزم أن يكون العلم بكونه قديماً ضرورياً، ولما بطل هذا فسد هذا القسم، وإنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قدم الجسم زائدا على كونه جسما؛ لأن ذلك الزائد إن كان قديما لزم أن يكون قدمه زائدا عليه، ولزم التسلسل، وإن كان حادثا فكل حادث فله أول وكل قديم فلا أول له فلو كان قدم القديم عبارة عن ذلك الحادث لزم أن يكون ذلك الشيء له أول وأن لا يكون له أول، وهو محال. فإن عارضونا بكونه حادثا قلنا : الحادث عبارة عن مجموع الوجود الحاصل في الحال والعدم السابق ولا يبعد حصول العلم بالوجود الحاصل مع الجهل بالعدم السابق، بخلاف القديم فإنه لا معنى له إلا نفس وجوده فظهر الفرق، وهذا وجه جليل، وفيه مباحث دقيقة. وليكن هذا آخر كلامنا في شرح دلائل حدوث الأجسام والله أعلم.
The fifth proof of the temporality of bodies: If the body were eternal, its eternity would either be identical to its being a body or additional to its being a body. Both divisions are false, so the claim that the body is eternal is invalid. We say: It is not permissible for the eternity of the body to be identical to its being a body, because if that were the case, then knowledge of its being a body would be knowledge of its being eternal. So just as knowledge of its being a body is necessary (intuitive), it would follow that knowledge of its being eternal is necessary. But since this is false, this division is invalid. And we say: It is not permissible for the eternity of the body to be additional to its being a body, because that additional (quality), if it is eternal, then its eternity would be additional to it, leading to an infinite regress. And if it is originated, then everything originated has a beginning, while everything eternal has no beginning. So if the eternity of the eternal were that originated thing, it would follow that that thing has a beginning and does not have a beginning, which is absurd. If they object by saying it (the additional quality) is originated, we say: The originated thing consists of a combination of existence occurring at the present moment and prior non-existence. It is not unlikely to have knowledge of the existence found while being ignorant of the prior non-existence. Unlike the eternal, for which there is no meaning other than its very existence. Thus the difference becomes clear. This is a profound point, and it contains subtle investigations. Let this be the end of our words in explaining the proofs of the temporality of bodies, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that bodies are originated. Razi argues by examining whether the eternity of a body is identical to its being a body or a separate quality. The first option fails because if eternity were identical to bodyhood, then our intuitive knowledge that a body is a body would entail knowledge of its eternity, which is not the case. The second option fails because the additional quality would either be eternal (leading to infinite regress) or originated (leading to contradiction). Razi also addresses a counterargument about originated qualities, clarifying that originated things combine existence with prior non-existence, unlike the eternal which is pure existence. The proof is considered profound.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is a body, it must be eternal.
Correction: Being a body does not imply eternity; our knowledge of bodyhood is separate from knowledge of temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility Razi considers for the eternity of a body?
A) Identical to its being a body
B) Additional to its being a body
C) Inherent to its soul
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
The eternity of a body cannot be identical to its bodyhood because that would make knowledge of eternity necessary, which it is not.

Fifth Proof: Second Division and Objection
Razi rejects the additional quality option and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an additional eternal attribute leads to infinite regress or contradiction, and how Razi answers an objection.

Core Idea
An additional eternal attribute leads to regress; an originated one leads to contradiction.

Razi continues the proof by examining the second possibility: that eternity is an additional quality. He shows it leads to infinite regress if eternal, or contradiction if originated, and then addresses a counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the continuation of the proof, focusing on the rejection of the second division and the objection.
2
Analyze the second division
Razi considers the possibility that eternity is an additional quality. He then examines two sub-options: the quality being eternal or originated.
Both lead to absurdity.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
If the additional quality is eternal, then its eternity would be additional, leading to an infinite chain.
This is a classic regress argument.
4
Examine the objection and Razi's answer
The opponent may claim the quality is originated. Razi clarifies the difference between originated and eternal things.
This is a profound point.

The fifth proof of the temporality of bodies: If the body were eternal, its eternity would either be identical to its being a body or additional to its being a body. Both divisions are false, so the claim that the body is eternal is invalid. We say: It is not permissible for the eternity of the body to be identical to its being a body, because if that were the case, then knowledge of its being a body would be knowledge of its being eternal. So just as knowledge of its being a body is necessary (intuitive), it would follow that knowledge of its being eternal is necessary. But since this is false, this division is invalid. Let this be the end of our words in explaining the proofs of the temporality of bodies, and Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that bodies are originated. Razi argues by examining whether the eternity of a body is identical to its being a body or a separate quality. The First Argument for the Eternity of the World
Razi presents the first argument for the eternity of the world based on causal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by proponents of the world's eternity, which is based on the necessity of causal conditions being eternally present.

Core Idea
If God's causal conditions are eternal, the world must also be eternal.

Razi begins his presentation of the arguments for the eternity of the world by outlining a key philosophical claim: if all the necessary conditions for God to act as an effectual agent were present from eternity, then the effect (the world) must itself be eternal. This argument challenges the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo. The passage is cut off, but the core premise is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi is not stating his own view but reporting the argument of those who believe in the eternity of the world.
The text ends abruptly; the argument is incomplete but the main premise is stated.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument says: if every necessary condition for God to be effective (mu'aththir) was already present in pre-eternity (azal), then the effect must follow necessarily.
This is a causal principle: given all conditions, the effect cannot be delayed.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
If the world is the effect of God's action and all conditions for that action were eternal, then the world itself must be eternal. This directly opposes the Islamic belief that the world was created in time.
Think about how a mutakallim might respond to this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بالقدم بوجوه : أولها : لا شك أن الممكنات تنتهي في سلسلة الحاجة إلى واجب الوجود، فنقول : كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرًا في آثاره إما أن يقال إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. فإن كان كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية حاصلا في الأزل، فإما أن يجب مع
And the proponents of eternity argued with several points: The first of them: There is no doubt that contingents end in a chain of need to the Necessary of Existence. We say: Everything that is indispensable for Him to be effective in His effects, either it is said that it was present from eternity or it was not present from eternity. If everything indispensable for causality was present from eternity, then either it must necessarily [occur] with [it?]... (text incomplete).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage begins the presentation of arguments for the eternity of the world. The first argument is based on the idea that once all necessary conditions for the action of the Necessary Being are present from eternity, the effect must also be eternal. The argument is cut off, but it introduces the classical kalām objection to the position that the world is created ex nihilo. Razi does not answer it here; he will likely refute it in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
قدم
مؤثر
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is Razi's own view.
Correction: Razi is presenting the opponents' argument; he will refute it later in the text.

Checkpoint

A) Contingent things are eternal.
B) The necessary conditions for God's effect are eternal, so the effect must be eternal.
C) The world is self-sufficient.
D) God's attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The eternity argument relies on the idea that if all causal conditions are eternal, the effect must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument claims that if all conditions were present from eternity, the world must be eternal. What condition might that be, and how does it affect your understanding of God's freedom?
?

?

The Eternalists' Objection to Temporal Creation
Imam Razi presents the first argument of the eternalists with a detailed logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eternalists' argument for the world's eternity and why it challenges the theologians.

Core Idea
The eternalists' argument uses the principle of sufficient reason to demand a reason for creation at a specific time.

This lesson presents the central objection of those who believe the world is eternal. They argue that if God is eternal and all conditions for creation are met in eternity, then the world must also be eternal. Any attempt to specify a time for creation leads to a contradiction or an infinite regress. This argument is the most powerful challenge to the doctrine of temporal creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through to get the flow of the argument. Then review the literal translation to ensure you follow the logical steps.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main branches
The argument splits into two cases: either all conditions for agency were present in eternity or they were not. Note how each branch leads to a problematic conclusion.
The first branch leads to either necessary perpetuity or a contradiction; the second leads to infinite regress.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
In the first branch, if the effect is not necessary, then specification of a time requires either an additional cause (contradiction) or arbitrary preference (absurd). Grasp why both are unacceptable.
4
Recognize the infinite regress argument
If conditions are not all present in eternity, then the coming-to-be of those conditions requires another agent, leading to an endless chain. See why this is considered the definitive cornerstone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بالقدم بوجوه : أولها : لا شك أن الممكنات تنتهي في سلسلة الحاجة إلى واجب الوجود، فنقول : كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرًا في آثاره إما أن يقال إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. فإن كان كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية حاصلا في الأزل، فإما أن يجب مع حصولها حصول الأثر أو لا يجب، فإن وجب لزم من دوامه دوام الأثر، وإن لم يجب فلنفرض ذاته مع مجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، تارة مع وجود تلك الآثار وأخرى مع عدمها، فاختصاص ذلك الوقت بالوجود دون الوقت الآخر إما أن يتوقف على اختصاصه بأمر ما لأجله كان هو أولى بوجود ذلك الأثر، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان الأول كان ذلك المخصص معتبرًا في المؤثرية، وهو ما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الوقت فإذن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، وكنا قد فرضناه حاصلاً، هذا خلف، وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح أصلا، وهو محال، هذا إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية كان حاصلا في الأزل، أما إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان حاصلا في الأزل فحدوثه في لا يزال بعد أن لم يكن، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر أو لا يفتقر ، فإن لم يفتقر فقد حدث الممكن لا عن مرجح وإن افتقر نقلنا الكلام إلى كيفية إحداث تلك الأمور، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو هذا هو العمدة الكبرى للقوم. أجاب المتكلمون عنه في وجوه : أحدها: أنك لمَ لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم إنما حدث في الوقت المعين لأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاده في ذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات وليس لأحد أن يقول : لم تعلقت الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، ولم تتعلق إرادته بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ وذلك لأن تلك الإرادة بعينها وماهيتها المخصوصة اقتضت التعلق بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، والماهيات لا تعلل. ثانيها : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات، فكان علمه القديم متعلقا بأن العالم في أي الأوقات يوجد، وفي أيها لا يوجد والإرادة لا تتعلق بالشيء إلا على وفق العلم، فلهذا السبب تعلقت إرادة الله بإحداث العالم في الوقت الذي علم وقوعه فيه، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات. وثالثها : لِمَ لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذلك الوقت اختص بحكمة خفية لأجلها خصص الله تعالى إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات؟ وإذا كان الاحتمال قائمًا سقطت هذه المطالبة. ورابعها أن إحداث العالم في الأزل محال لأن الإحداث عبارة عن جعله
And those who hold eternity argued with arguments: First: There is no doubt that contingent things end in the chain of need to the Necessary Existent. We say: Everything that is indispensable for His being an agent upon His effects—either it is said that it was existent in pre-eternity or it was not existent in pre-eternity. If everything indispensable for agency was existent in pre-eternity, then either the existence of the effect is necessary along with its existence or not. If necessary, then from its perpetuity the perpetuity of the effect follows. If not, then let us assume His essence together with the totality of the things considered in agency, sometimes with the existence of those effects and sometimes with their non-existence. The specification of that time for existence over another time either depends on its being specified by something for which it is more deserving of the existence of that effect, or not. If the first, then that specification is considered in agency, and it is what was existent before that time. Then everything indispensable for agency was existent in pre-eternity, and we had assumed it existent—this is a contradiction. If the second, then that would be a preference of one of the two equal sides of the possible over the other without any preferent at all, which is absurd. This is if we say everything indispensable for agency was existent in pre-eternity. As for if we say that everything that was existent in pre-eternity occurs in [؟] after its non-existence, then it either needs an agent or not. If it does not need, then a possible event occurs without a preferent; if it needs, we transfer the speech to how those things are brought about, and an infinite regress follows. This is the definitive cornerstone of the school. The theologians answered it in several ways: First: Why is it not permissible to say that knowledge only occurs at the specified time because God's will was attached to bringing it into existence at that time rather than other times? And no one can ask why the will was attached to creating the world at that time and not at other times, because that particular will, by its own essence and specific quiddity, required attachment to creating the world at that time, and quiddities are not caused. Second: God is knowing of all particulars, so His eternal knowledge was attached to when the world would exist and when it would not, and will does not attach to anything except in accordance with knowledge. For this reason, God's will attached to creating the world at the time He knew it would occur, and did not attach to creating it at other times. Third: Why is it not permissible to say that that time was distinguished by a hidden wisdom for which God specified creating the world at that time rather than other times? And when the possibility stands, this demand falls. Fourth: Creating the world in pre-eternity is impossible, because creation is making it...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the main objection of the proponents of eternity and the theologians' responses. The objection is a sophisticated argument from the principle of sufficient reason: if God is eternal and His agency is complete in eternity, then the world should also be eternal. If not, there must be a specification, leading to either a vicious regress or arbitrary choice. The theologians give four answers: 1) The specification is due to God's will, which is not subject to further questioning—quiddities are uncaused. 2) God's eternal knowledge determines the appropriate time for creation, and will follows knowledge. 3) There may be a hidden wisdom that humans cannot grasp. 4) (incomplete) Creating the world in eternity is impossible because creation implies bringing into existence from non-existence, which cannot be eternal. The four answers collectively defend the position that the world is created in time.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
مؤثرية (agency)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is simplistic and easily dismissed.
Correction: The argument is sophisticated and forced the theologians to develop multiple responses.

Checkpoint
What does the eternalists' argument conclude if all conditions for agency were present in eternity and the effect is not necessary?
A) The world is eternal
B) A contradiction or arbitrary preference follows
C) Infinite regress follows
D) The argument fails

Key Takeaway
The eternalists' objection forces the theologians to provide a reason for why creation occurred at a particular time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to consider why the world was created at a specific time rather than earlier or later. Do you truly believe that your own existence at this moment in history is deliberate, or do you sometimes feel like a random outcome? How does this lesson challenge or affirm your sense of purpose?
?
The eternalists accuse those who believe in temporal creation of having no sufficient reason for the timing. If you were asked to defend your belief that Allah created the universe at a particular moment, would you be able to give a rational answer? Or would you fall back on 'Allah willed it' without further explanation? How does this make you feel about the strength of your conviction?
?
Consider the logical conclusion of this argument: if you accept it, you must either believe the world is eternal or face an infinite regress. Do you feel the weight of this intellectual challenge? Does it trouble you, or does your trust in revelation provide a peace that logic cannot shake? Reflect honestly on whether you are relying on reason alone or on a deeper certainty from faith.

The Theologians' Responses to the Eternity Argument
Imam Razi enumerates the theologians' four answers to the eternalists' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main theological responses to the eternalists' objection and how each addresses the issue of specification.

Core Idea
The theologians offer four distinct answers: God's will, knowledge, hidden wisdom, and the impossibility of eternal creation.

In response to the powerful objection of the eternalists, the theologians proposed multiple answers. These answers avoid reducing God's action to arbitrary choice or infinite regress. They appeal to divine will (which is not subject to external causation), divine knowledge (which determines the appropriate time), hidden wisdom (which humans may not grasp), and the logical impossibility of creation in eternity. This lesson presents these answers as found in Imam Razi's text.

Guided Path
1
Read the four answers
Read the Arabic passage containing the four answers. Notice how each answer is introduced by 'أحدها', 'ثانيها', etc. Then read the translation to see the different strategies.
2
Understand the first answer: will and quiddities
The first answer states that God's will, by its own essence, attaches to the specific time, and quiddities are not caused. This blocks the demand for an external reason.
Quiddities are the 'whatness' of things; they are not subject to causal explanation.
3
Understand the second and third answers
The second answer links God's eternal knowledge to His will: will follows knowledge of the best time. The third answer appeals to a hidden wisdom beyond human grasp. Both provide a rationale without revealing details.
4
Reflect on the fourth answer
The fourth answer argues that creation in eternity is impossible because 'creation' implies temporal origination. Note that the text is incomplete but the idea is clear.
This answer targets the very definition of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بالقدم بوجوه : أولها : لا شك أن الممكنات تنتهي في سلسلة الحاجة إلى واجب الوجود، فنقول : كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثرًا في آثاره إما أن يقال إنه كان حاصلا في الأزل أو ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. فإن كان كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية حاصلا في الأزل، فإما أن يجب مع حصولها حصول الأثر أو لا يجب، فإن وجب لزم من دوامه دوام الأثر، وإن لم يجب فلنفرض ذاته مع مجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ وذلك لأن تلك الإرادة بعينها وماهيتها المخصوصة اقتضت التعلق بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، والماهيات لا تعلل. ثانيها : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات، فكان علمه القديم متعلقا بأن The theologians provide multiple plausible explanations for why God created the world at a specific time, preserving both divine wisdom and the world's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a delay or unexpected timing in your life, do you trust that God's hidden wisdom is at work, or do you demand an explanation from Him? How can you cultivate patience and reliance on His knowledge?
?
The theologians argued that God's will is not subject to external causes. In your own life, are you trying to 'cause' God's will by your actions, or are you submitting to His sovereign will even when you don't understand?
?
God's will follows His eternal knowledge. If you truly believe that God knows what is best for you, how should that change the way you make decisions in your daily life? Will you align your will with what He has revealed, even when it's difficult?

4 min
Six Answers Collapse into One Flawed Point
Razi concludes that six answers reduce to one already-refuted division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why six proposed answers to the argument for temporal creation all fail because they rely on a single already-refuted premise.

Core Idea
Six answers collapse into one flawed point already refuted by Razi.

Razi concludes his discussion by showing that multiple attempted objections to his proof all ultimately make the same mistake. He identifies one shared weak point that has already been demolished. This passage demonstrates how a careful argument can preemptively close off many lines of criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Razi's conclusion.
Notice the emphatic final phrase: 'ليست أجوبة عن تلك الحجة البتة أصلا'.
2
Identify the single point of convergence
Razi says all six answers reduce to one point: choosing the division that what is necessary for effecting was not present in pre-eternity. Find that phrase in the text.
3
Understand why that point fails
Razi refutes this choice with two possibilities: either the occurrence of those necessary things needed no preponderator (leading to denial of the Maker) or it needed a preponderator (leading to infinite regress). Trace this argument in the passage.
This shows that the original proof remains untouched.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت أن هذه الأجوبة الستة كلها راجعة إلى حرف واحد، وهو اختيار أحد القسمين المذكورين في تلك الحجة، وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان في الأزل، لكنا ذكرنا هذا القسم وأبطلناه بأن تلك الأمور لما لم تكن حاصلة في الأزل ثم حدثت، فإن كان حدوثها غنيا عن المرجح لزم نفي الصانع، وإن افتقر إلى المرجح عاد التقسيم الأول فيه، ولزم التسلسل، فهذه الوجوه ليست أجوبة عن تلك الحجة البتة أصلا.
Thus it is established that all of these six answers return to a single point, which is the choice of one of the two divisions mentioned in that proof: that everything necessary for effecting [the world] was not [present] in pre-eternity. But we mentioned this division and invalidated it because those things, when they were not present in pre-eternity and then occurred, if their occurrence was independent of a preponderator, then denial of the Maker follows; and if they were dependent on a preponderator, the first division returns in it, and infinite regress follows. So these are by no means answers to that proof at all.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and summary. Razi states that all six proposed answers to an earlier argument (presumably for the temporal creation of the world) converge on a single point: they choose the option that everything necessary for making the world exist was not present in pre-eternity. But Razi says that this option was already refuted: either those necessary things came to be without a preponderator, which denies the Maker, or they needed a preponderator, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, none of these six answers actually refute the original proof.

Key Terms
الأجوبة الستة (the six answers)
المؤثرية (effecting/agency)
المرجح (preponderator)
التسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the six answers are distinct and independent objections; Razi shows they all share the same flawed premise.
Correction: All six answers reduce to choosing one side of a division that Razi has already refuted. They are not independent but collapse into the same fatal flaw.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what do all six answers reduce to?
A) They all deny the Maker
B) They all choose one of two divisions in the original proof
C) They all involve infinite regress
D) They all accept that the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Six proposed answers to the proof for temporal creation collapse into a single already-refuted premise and therefore fail entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on multiple excuses or justifications for your beliefs, only to find they all share the same weak foundation? Will you examine your own reasons for faith to see if they collapse under scrutiny, or will you settle for comfort over truth?
?
Razi shows that the six answers lead to either denying the Maker or infinite regress. Are you willing to follow an argument to its uncomfortable conclusion, even if it exposes flaws in your own thinking?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself choosing intellectual shortcuts that avoid the full weight of the truth? What would it cost you to abandon those weak arguments and embrace a sounder foundation?

The Division Argument and al-Wajh al-Thalith
Imam Razi presents the division argument and the third argument for existence as a common concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from the division of existents and the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) that existence is a single concept shared by all existents.

Core Idea
Existence is a single concept, as shown by the division of existents and the stability of belief.

Imam Razi begins the second question by observing that the very division of existents into necessary and possible presupposes a common meaning. He then presents the third argument, which shows that our belief in a thing's existence remains unchanged even when we change our belief about its specific nature. This constancy indicates that existence is a single concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that the passage begins with a division and its implication.
2
Understand the division argument
The division of existents into necessary and possible requires a common basis for the division. That common basis is the concept of existence itself.
If existence were not common, the division would be impossible.
3
Analyze the third argument
Our belief that something exists does not change when we change our belief about its specific nature (substance/accident, etc.). This shows existence is a single concept.
Compare with how belief in substance changes when we later believe it is an accident.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments point to existence being a shared concept. Consider why this is important for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية. الموجود إلى الواجب لذاته وإلى الممكن لذاته، فمورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون المفهوم من الموجود قدرًا مشتركا بين القسمين. الوجه الثالث : إنا إذا اعتقدنا أن أمرًا من الأمور موجود فسواء اعتقدنا أن ذلك الأمر جوهر أو عرض، وبتقدير أن يكون جوهرًا فهو متحيز أو غير متحيز، وبتقدير أن يكون عرضا فهو إما لون أو طعم، فإن اعتقدنا في كونه موجودا يكون باقيا غير متغير، ولولا أن المفهوم من كونه موجودًا أمر واحد في الكل لوجب أن يتغير ذلك الاعتقاد عند تغير اعتقاد الخصوصيات كما إذا اعتقدنا فيه أنه جوهر، ثم اعتقدنا فيه عرض، فإنه يتغير الاعتقاد الأول. الوجه الرابع : أن من قال : الوجود غير مشترك فيه بين الماهيات يلزمه الاعتراف بأن الوجود مشترك فيه بين الماهيات وإلا لكنا نفتقر في كل واحد واحد من مسمى الوجود بأنه غير مشترك فيه بين الماهيات إلى دليل منفصل، ولما كان الحكم على الوجود بأنه غير مشترك فيه، كل وجود علمنا أن الوجود من حيث أنه وجود مفهوم واحد. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الوجود أمر واحد في جميع الموجودات، وأما أن خصوصية ماهية كل واحد من الماهيات غير مشترك فيها بين الماهيات فمعلوم بالضرورة، فثبت بمجموع هاتين المقدمتين أن وجود كل شيء مغاير لماهيته.
The second question. The existent is divided into the necessary in itself and the possible in itself, so the basis of division is common between the two divisions, and this requires that the meaning of 'existent' be a common factor between the two divisions. The third aspect: When we believe that a thing is existent, whether we believe that thing is a substance or an accident, and if it is a substance it is either spatial or non-spatial, and if it is an accident it is either color or taste, our belief in its being existent remains constant and unchanged. If the meaning of its being existent were not one thing in all cases, then that belief would necessarily change when the belief in the particularities changes, just as when we believe it is a substance and then believe it is an accident, the first belief changes. The fourth aspect: Whoever says that existence is not shared among essences is forced to admit that existence is shared among essences, otherwise we would need a separate proof for each and every thing called existence that it is not shared among essences. Since the judgment that existence is not shared among essences applies to every existence, we know that existence inasmuch as it is existence is one concept. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that existence is one thing in all existents. As for the particularity of the essence of each one of the essences not being shared among essences, that is known by necessity. So by combining these two premises it is established that the existence of every thing is other than its essence.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument that existence (wujud) is a common concept shared by all existents, and that it is additional to essence (mahiyya). The passage begins by stating that the division of existents into the Necessary in itself and the possible in itself presupposes a common basis for the division, which is the concept of existence itself. Then it presents two arguments (al-wajh al-thalith and al-wajh al-rabi). The third argument: Our belief that a thing is existent remains constant regardless of whether we later believe it is a substance or an accident, or specific kinds thereof. If the meaning of existence were not a single concept, our belief would change when the particularities change. Since it does not change, existence must be a single concept. The fourth argument: If someone claims that existence is not shared among essences, they are forced to admit that it is shared, because otherwise proving that each individual thing called 'existence' is not shared would require an infinite regress of proofs. But we directly know that existence as such is a single concept. The conclusion: existence is one in all existents, while the particularity of each essence is not shared; therefore existence is other than essence.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
قدر مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence has different meanings for different kinds of beings (e.g., God vs. creations).
Correction: The third argument shows that our belief in existence does not change when we change our beliefs about the specific nature, so existence is a single concept common to all existents.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith), what remains constant when we change our belief about a thing's specific nature?
A) Belief in its existence
B) Belief in its substance
C) Belief in its accident
D) Belief in its color

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single, common concept shared by all existents, as shown by the division of existents and the stability of belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your belief in your own existence never wavers, even when your identity changes—job, health, relationships. But do you truly believe that the same existence is shared by the Creator and His creation? If so, how does that reshape your daily awareness of Him? This week, pause each morning before speaking or acting, and silently affirm: 'My existence and His are both real, but His is necessary and mine is given.'
?
You constantly label things—important, trivial, sacred, mundane. Yet the lesson shows that the existence behind all labels is one. When you look at something you normally dismiss, can you see its existence as equally real as your own? Take one object you usually ignore today and say to yourself: 'Its existence is real, and it is a sign of the One Existent.'
?
If existence is a single concept, then your own existence is not separate from the existence of the universe or of God—it is a reflection of the same reality. Does this thought terrify you or liberate you? Commit today to writing one sentence: 'My existence is not mine, it is a gift from the One whose existence is necessary.'

The Fourth Argument and the Conclusion
Imam Razi presents the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rabi) and the concluding synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rabi) and how it, combined with the previous one, proves that existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
Denying that existence is shared leads to regress; we can judge existence as one, so it is shared and additional.

Imam Razi's fourth argument takes a different approach: it points out that denying that existence is shared among essences is self-defeating, because it would require a separate proof for each existent. Since we can judge existence as a single concept, it must be shared. This argument, together with the previous one, leads to the conclusion that existence is additional to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Read the Arabic and translation for al-wajh al-rabi. Focus on the reductio ad absurdum.
Notice the logical structure: if not shared, then infinite regress.
2
Understand the reductio
If existence were not shared, proving that each thing's existence is not shared would require an infinite chain of proofs. But we can judge existence as a single concept.
This is a foundational argument in Islamic intellectual history.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Combine this with the earlier point that existence is common, and the fact that essences are distinct, to conclude existence is other than essence.
The particularity of each essence is not shared, so existence must be additional.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the two arguments (third and fourth) work together to establish the famous doctrine that existence is additional to essence.
This is a key distinction in ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third aspect: When we believe that a thing is existent, whether we believe that thing is a substance or an accident, and if it is a substance it is either spatial or non-spatial, and if it is an accident it is either color or taste, our belief in its being existent remains constant and unchanged. ?
The argument shows that your existence is not the same as your essence—your 'you-ness' is unique, but the fact that you exist is a concept shared with every other thing. If your existence is not intrinsic to you, then you owe your existence to another. Does that truth make you feel dependent or grateful? How would you live differently today if you truly felt that every moment of your existence is a gift rather than a given?
?
If existence is one concept, then your existence is no more or less 'existence' than that of a rock or an angel. Does that humble you? In what ways do you act as if your existence is more significant or self-sufficient? What would change if you accepted that your being is simply a share in a common reality, not a private possession?
?
Imam al-Razi proves that we can make a universal judgment about existence without needing separate proofs for each instance. This implies that there is objective knowledge about reality that is not tied to individual cases. Are there areas in your life where you refuse to accept universal truths because they challenge your personal preferences? What would it cost you to submit to such a truth today?
