The Argument from Possibility: Essence Distinct from Existence
Imam Razi argues that possibility proves essence is distinct from existence, with an objection and two-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the concept of possibility (imkan) to prove that existence is an additional attribute to essence, and how he defends this proof against an objection.

Core Idea
Possibility shows essence is distinct from existence because essence must remain constant across existence and non-existence.

Imam Razi presents his second proof for the distinction between existence and essence, grounded in the nature of possible beings. The argument carefully considers the states of existence and non-existence, then addresses a nuanced objection based on temporal possibility. This lesson unpacks the logical steps and the two-part rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the first part where Razi considers a possible thing under existence and under non-existence. Notice that each state excludes the other, so the essence must be other than both.
The key is that possibility is not found in existence or non-existence themselves.
2
Understand the alternative formulation
The second version uses the idea of judgment: saying a thing is possible means its essence can be judged as existent and as non-existent, requiring the essence to be established in both states.
This highlights that the subject of the judgment must persist.
3
Identify the objection
The objector claims that possibility is only about the future, not the present state. They argue that the proof only works if possibility is attributed to the present moment.
This objection challenges the assumption that essence is judged now.
4
Study the first part of the answer
The first answer shows that even future non-existence involves judging the present essence, which must be able to be without existence in the future, proving distinction.
Reason's judgment about the future still requires a subject.
5
Study the second part of the answer
The second answer dissects the objector's claim: the possibility of future non-existence cannot be actual now, so it must be actual only when the future arrives—which then becomes present, so possibility is again about the present.
This refutes the objection by showing it leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أن الوجود مغاير للماهية هي أنه لا شك أن في الموجودات ما هو ممكن لذاته، فنقول : ذلك الموجود الذي هو ممكن لذاته إن اعتبار الوجود كان غير قابل للعدم ؛ لأن الشيء حال كونه موجودًا لا يقبل العدم، وما لا يقبل العدم لا يكون ممكن الوجود والعدم، وإذا أخذناه مع اعتبار العدم كان غير قابل للوجود ؛ لأن الشيء حال كونه معدوما لا يقبل الوجود، وما لا يقبل الوجود لا يكون ممكن الوجود والعدم، فلو لم تكن ماهيته مغايرة للوجود والعدم لما كانت الماهية ممكنة أصلاً، ولما كانت ممكنة، علمنا أنها مغايرة للوجود والعدم. ويمكن تقرير هذه الحجة بعبارة أخرى وهي أن قولنا في الشيء إنه ممكن الوجود والعدم أنه لا يمتنع أن يحكم على تلك الماهية بكونها موجودة وبكونها معدومة، والمحكوم عليه بحكم لا بد وأن يكون متقررًا ذلك الحكم فوجب أن تكون تلك الماهية الممكنة متقررة حالتي الوجود والعدم، وذلك يقتضي كون الماهية مغايرة للوجود. فإن قيل : هذه الحجة إنما تلزم لو قلنا : الماهية حال وجودها، أو حال عدمها يكون محكوما عليها في ذلك الوقت بأنها ممكنة الوجود، نحن لا نقول بذلك، بل نزعم أنها حال وجودها يمكن وجودها وعمها في الزمان الثاني من ذلك الزمان الحاضر، والحاصل أنا لا نسلم ثبوت الإمكان بالنسبة إلى الحال، بل نسلم ثبوته بالنسبة إلى زمان الاستقبال. والجواب من وجهين : الأول : أن الموجود في الحال إذا حكم العقل عليه بأنه يمكن أن يصير معدومًا في الاستقبال، فالمحكوم عليه بالعدم في الاستقبال هو هذه الماهية والمحكوم عليه يحكم لا بد وأن يكون متقررًا مع ذلك الحكم، فتلك الماهية ممكنة التقرر مع العدم في الاستقبال، وإذا حصلت الماهية بدون الوجود كانت الماهية مغايرة للوجود. الثاني : هو أن قولكم الشيء يمكن أن يصير معدوما في الاستقبال يحتمل وجهين : أحدهما : أن إمكان العدم في الاستقبال حاصل في الحال. الآخر أن إمكان العدم في الاستقبال يحصل في الاستقبال. والأول باطل لأن إمكان العدم في الاستقبال مشروط بحصول الاستقبال، والاستقبال ممتنع الحصول في الحال فإمكان العدم في الاستقبال موقوف على شرط ممتنع الحصول لذاته، وما توقف على هذا الشرط كان ممتنع الحصول في الحال، فثبت أنه يمتنع أن يحصل في الحال إمكان العدم في الاستقبال، ولما بطل هذا الاحتمال بقي الاحتمال الثاني وهو أن الموجود في الحال يمكن أن يصير معدوما في الاستقبال عند حضور الاستقبال، لكن الاستقبال إذا حضر صار حاضرًا، فحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى أن الممكن إنما يكون محكوما عليه بالإمكان بالنسبة إلى الحال، لا بالنسبة إلى الاستقبال، وذلك يبطل ما ذكرتم من السؤال.
The second proof that existence is other than essence is that there is no doubt that among existents there is what is possible in itself. We say: That existent which is possible in itself, when considered with existence, is not receptive to non-existence because a thing in the state of being existent does not accept non-existence, and what does not accept non-existence is not possible of existence and non-existence. And when we take it with the consideration of non-existence, it is not receptive to existence because a thing in the state of being non-existent does not accept existence, and what does not accept existence is not possible of existence and non-existence. So if its essence were not other than existence and non-existence, the essence would not be possible at all. And since it is possible, we know that it is other than existence and non-existence. This proof can be formulated in another expression: our saying about a thing that it is possible of existence and non-existence means that it is not impossible to judge that essence as being existent and as being non-existent. The object of a judgment must be established along with that judgment, so that possible essence must be established in both states of existence and non-existence, and this requires that the essence be other than existence. If it is said: This proof only follows if we say that the essence in the state of its existence or in the state of its non-existence is judged at that time as being possible of existence. We do not say that; rather we claim that in the state of its existence it can exist and [؟] in the second time from that present time. In short, we do not affirm the actuality of possibility relative to the present, but we affirm its actuality relative to the future time. The answer is from two aspects: First: When reason judges that a present existent can become non-existent in the future, the object judged as non-existent in the future is this essence, and the object of a judgment must be established with that judgment. So that essence is capable of being established with non-existence in the future, and if the essence obtains without existence, then the essence is other than existence. Second: Your statement that a thing can become non-existent in the future admits two possibilities: one: that the possibility of non-existence in the future is actual in the present. The other: that the possibility of non-existence in the future obtains in the future. The first is false because the possibility of non-existence in the future is conditional upon the occurrence of the future, and the future is impossible to obtain in the present, so the possibility of non-existence in the future depends on a condition that is impossible in itself. Whatever depends on such a condition is impossible to obtain in the present. So it is established that it is impossible for the possibility of non-existence in the future to obtain in the present. When this possibility is invalidated, the second possibility remains: that the present existent can become non-existent in the future when the future arrives. But when the future arrives, it becomes present, so then the matter returns to the possible being judged by possibility relative to the present, not relative to the future. This invalidates your objection.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that existence is additional to essence, based on the concept of possibility (imkan). The proof begins by noting that some existents are possible in themselves (mumkin li-dhatihi). For such an existent, when considered with existence, it does not accept non-existence because a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent at the same time. Similarly, when considered with non-existence, it does not accept existence. So a possible thing considered in itself must be something that is capable of both existence and non-existence; this capacity is not found in existence itself or non-existence itself. Therefore, the essence (mahiyya) of the possible thing must be other than both existence and non-existence. An alternative formulation is then given: when we say a thing is possible, we mean that it is not impossible to judge its essence as being existent and as being non-existent. The object of a judgment must be established at the time of the judgment, so the essence must be established in both states, which requires it to be distinct from existence. An objection is raised: this proof only works if we claim that the essence is judged as possible at the very moment it is existent or non-existent. But we (the objectors) say that possibility is not about the present state; rather, a thing is possible in the sense that it can exist or not exist in the future. The answer has two parts. The first part argues that when reason judges that a present existent can become non-existent in the future, the essence is still the subject of that future judgment; it must be established with that judgment, so the essence can be without existence in the future, which again shows essence is distinct from existence. The second part dissects the objector's claim: if they say 'the thing can become non-existent in the future', this possibility must either be actual in the present or only in the future. The first is impossible because future possibility depends on the future arriving, which cannot happen now. So it must be that the possibility is only in the future, but when the future arrives it becomes present, so the judgment of possibility is again about the present, not the future. This refutes the objection and reaffirms the proof.

Key Terms
ممكن لذاته (possible in itself)
إمكان (possibility)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
ماهية (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think possibility (imkan) is only about the future, not about the present state, so the argument that essence must be distinct from existence in the present does not hold.
Correction: Razi shows that even if possibility is attributed to the future, the essence must still be the subject of that future judgment, requiring it to be distinct from existence. Moreover, the notion of 'future possibility' necessarily collapses to present possibility when the future arrives, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
In the second argument, the objection claims that possibility (imkan) is only relative to the future, not the present. How does the first part of the answer respond?
A) It says the future never arrives.
B) It argues that the essence is still subject to the judgment of possible non-existence in the future, requiring essence to be distinct from existence.
C) It denies possibility altogether.
D) It asserts that existence can become non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's second proof demonstrates that because a possible being can be conceived as existent and non-existent, its essence must be distinct from both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is distinct from your existence, then who you truly are is not defined by your current circumstances. Are you living in awareness of your unchanging essence as created by Allah, or are you consumed by transient states of existence? Will you seek to know your essence through His revelation?
?
The possibility of non-existence is real for you at every moment. Does this urgency shape your priorities, or do you live as if your existence is guaranteed? How would you change your today if you knew it could be your last?
?
Imam Razi argues that your essence is established even when you are not existent. How does that affect your understanding of Allah's knowledge of you before your birth and after your death? Are you comfortable being known by Him in both states, and does that motivate you to live righteously now?

Third Argument: Conceiving Essence Without Existence
Imam Razi presents the argument that we can conceive essence while ignoring its existence, proving existence is additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third proof that existence is additional to essence, based on our ability to conceive an essence while ignoring its existence.

Core Idea
We can think of a thing without caring if it exists — proving existence is an extra addition.

Imam Razi presents a simple yet powerful argument: we can conceive the essence (māhiyya) of a thing while being utterly heedless of whether it actually exists. If existence were identical to or part of the essence, this would be impossible. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a concise proof statement.
2
Unpack the logic
Identify the key components: the ability to conceive essence heedless of existence, and the conditional statement that if existence were part of essence, this would be impossible. Then derive the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument proves that existence is additional (zā'id) to essence, not identical or part of it. Think of concrete examples like a unicorn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية وهو أنه يمكننا أن نعقل الماهية مع الذهول عن وجودها ولو كان الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزءًا من أجزاء الماهية لكان ذلك ممتنعا.
The third proof that existence is additional to essence is that we can conceive the essence while being heedless of its existence. If existence were the very essence or a part of the essence, that would be impossible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This short passage presents the third proof that existence is additional to essence (zā'id 'alā al-māhiyya). The proof is simple: we can conceive (na'qil) an essence while being heedless (al-dhuhlūl) of its existence. For example, we can think of a unicorn without considering whether it actually exists. If existence were identical to the essence or a part of it, then it would be impossible to conceive the essence while ignoring its existence, because the essence would necessarily include existence. Since we clearly can do so, existence must be something additional to the essence, not identical or part of it.

Key Terms
عقل
الذهول عن الوجود
زائد على الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conceiving a thing without existence is impossible because existence is a necessary property.
Correction: The argument shows that we can conceive the quiddity itself, not the property of existence, so existence is not part of the quiddity.

Checkpoint
The third argument proves that existence is additional to essence because:
A) We can conceive essence while ignoring its existence, which would be impossible if existence were part of essence.
B) Existence is the same as essence.
C) We cannot conceive essence without existence.
D) Existence is a part of essence.

Key Takeaway
The ability to conceive an essence while ignoring its existence proves that existence is not identical to or part of that essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can conceive of Allah's existence while being heedless of its implications. What specific area of your life would change if you stopped mentally ignoring that He is actually present and watching?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you merely conceive the meaning of the words, or do you actively affirm the existence of Allah in that moment? What is one practical step to move from heedless conception to living awareness?
?
Think of a sin you commit while knowing its essence (what it is) but choosing to ignore its existent reality in front of Allah. What would it take for you to let that knowledge transform your action today?

Three Objections to Existence Being Additional to Essence
Razi enumerates three objections against the additionalness thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key objections raised against the claim that existence is additional to essence, and their logical structure.

Core Idea
Objections exploit regress, mental vs external existence, and confusion between identity and conception.

In theological debates, every argument faces objections. Imam Razi presents three sophisticated challenges to the proof that existence is additional to essence. These objections tap into deep issues about conception, assent, and the nature of existence. Understanding them is crucial to appreciating Razi's defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how each objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل' or 'وأيضًا'.
The objections are sequential and build on each other.
2
Identify the logical flaw in each objection
For each objection, determine what it claims is impossible (e.g., regress, contradiction) and why it targets the additionalness thesis.
The first objection uses the philosophers' view of God; the second uses the conception-assent relation; the third uses relations and mental existence.
3
Reflect on the cumulative force of the objections
Consider why these three objections together seem to undermine the proof. Ask yourself: Can the additionalness thesis survive?
The answer will come in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية
فإن قيل : هذه حجة منقوضة على قول الفلاسفة؛ فإن عندهم وجود الباري سبحانه نفس حقيقته، مع أنا نعقل وجوده ولا نعقل ماهيته.
وأيضًا فإنا نتصور أن الوجود ما هو ؟ ثم نصدق بأن ذلك المفهوم حاصل وكائن واقع والتصور في التصديق فيلزم أن يكون تصديقنا بأن الوجود واقع وحاصل دالا على حصول وجود، ويلزم منه التسلسل
وأيضًا: فإنا قد نعقل ذاتين مع الذهول عن كون أحدهما لازما للآخر، أو ملزوما له، أو كون أحدهما مؤثرًا في الآخر، أو أثرًا له، أو كون أحدهما حالا في الآخر، أو محلا له فيلزم أن يكون كون الشيء لازما للآخر وملزوما له، ومؤثرًا فيه وأثرًا له، وحالا فيه ومحلا له زائدًا على الذات، وذلك محال لإفضاء ذلك إلى التسلسل، وأيضًا فهب أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن الوجود الخارجي زائد على الماهية ولكنه لا يفيد أن الوجود الذهني زائد على الماهية.
والجواب : أنه إذا صدق على أحد الأمرين كونه معلومًا وعلى الآخر أنه غير معلوم فلو لم يثبت التغير بينهما، لكان قد صدق على الأمر الواحد أنه معلوم وأنه غير معلوم فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات وإنه محال في بدائه العقول، وإذا كانت هذه المقدمة من أقوى البدهيات كان إيراد النقص عليها تشكيكاً، فلا يستحق الجواب. وأيضا : فعندنا أن وجود الله زائد على ماهيته وأما تصديقنا بأن الوجود قد وقع فليس المراد منه أن الوجود حصل له وجود أخر، بل المراد منه أن الوجود هل حصل للماهية أم لا ؟ وهذا عين الدليل الذي تمسكنا به وأما حديث اللازمية والملزومية وأمثالها فهي اعتبارات ذهنية بخلاف الوجود الخارجي، فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه اعتبار ذهني، وإلا لزم أن يقال إنه لا موجود في الأعيان، وأن يقال: وجوده في الأعيان نفس الماهية فحينئذ يرجع ما ذكرتم من اجتماع النفي والإثبات على الشيء الواحد، وهو محال. وأما الوجود الذهني فجوابه أن الماهية كما وجدت في الأذهان عارية عن الوجود الخارجي، فقد وجدت في الأعيان عارية عن الوجود الذهني، وذلك يوجب التغاير.
The Second Issue. If it is said: This argument is refuted according to the philosophers; for with them the existence of the Exalted Creator is identical to His essence, while we conceive His existence and do not conceive His quiddity. Also, we conceive what existence is, then we assent that that concept is actual, occurring, and real, and conception is prior to assent, so our assent that existence is actual and occurring must indicate the obtaining of existence, which leads to infinite regress. Also, we may conceive two essences while being heedless of whether one is necessary for the other or the other is necessitated by it, or whether one is an effect in the other or an effect of it, or whether one is inhering in the other or a locus for it; so it follows that a thing's being necessary for another and necessitated by it, effective in it and an effect of it, inhering in it and a locus for it, is additional to the essence, and that is impossible because it leads to infinite regress. Also, even if what you mention indicates that external existence is additional to quiddity, it does not show that mental existence is additional to quiddity. The answer: If one of two things is characterized as known and the other as unknown, then if there were no difference between them, the single thing would be characterized as known and unknown, necessitating the conjunction of negation and affirmation, and that is impossible in the primality of minds. Since this premise is among the strongest self-evident truths, raising a refutation against it is skepticism, which does not deserve an answer. Also, with us, God's existence is additional to His quiddity. As for our assent that existence has occurred, its meaning is not that existence has another existence, but rather whether existence has occurred for the quiddity or not; this is precisely the proof we have adduced. As for the talk of necessity and necessitation and the like, they are mental considerations, unlike external existence, for it cannot be said that it is a mental consideration; otherwise it would follow that there is no existent in external reality, and it would be said that its existence in external reality is the very quiddity; then what you mentioned of the conjunction of negation and affirmation regarding the same thing would follow, and that is impossible. As for mental existence, its answer is that just as the quiddity is found in minds stripped of external existence, so it is found in external reality stripped of mental existence, and this necessitates difference.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. Razi presents three objections against the proof that existence is additional to quiddity (essence), then gives a composite answer. The first objection: Philosophers hold that God's existence is identical to His essence, yet we conceive His existence without knowing His quiddity—implying existence is not additional. The second: Conception of existence precedes assent to its reality, leading to infinite regress if assent indicates existence. The third: We can conceive two essences without knowing relations (necessity, causation, inherence), so those relations are additional, but that leads to regress; also the proof only covers external existence, not mental existence. Razi's answer begins with a self-evident principle: if one thing is known and another unknown, they cannot be identical; otherwise a single thing would be both known and unknown, which is contradictory. Since this is a strong self-evident truth, the objection is dismissed as skepticism. Then he clarifies: God's existence is indeed additional to His essence (contrary to the philosophers). Our assent that 'existence has occurred' does not mean existence gets another existence, but rather that existence attaches to quiddity—exactly what the proof shows. The relations of necessity etc. are mental considerations, not real additions like external existence. If external existence were merely mental, external reality would be empty. Regarding mental existence, the answer is straightforward: quiddity can exist in the mind without external existence, and externally without mental existence, so they differ. Thus the objections are refuted.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyyah)
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objections prove that existence is never additional to essence.
Correction: The objections are refuted by Razi's answer (next lesson). They highlight challenges but do not disprove the additionalness thesis once properly understood.

Checkpoint
Which objection relies on the idea that conception precedes assent (tasawwur qabl al-tasdiq)?
A) The first objection (philosophers' view)
B) The second objection (regress from conception and assent)
C) The third objection (relations and mental existence)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three objections target the additionalness thesis by highlighting regress, mental vs external existence, and confusion between identity and conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you conceive of your own existence, do you instinctively separate 'you' from the fact that you exist? How does this lesson challenge you to examine whether you treat your essence and existence as distinct or identical in your daily self-awareness?
?
The second objection highlights that affirming a concept as real can lead to a regress if the concept itself must already be realized. Have you ever experienced a similar intellectual unease when trying to prove something foundational about your faith? How will you guard against such infinite regresses in your own reasoning about God?
?
The philosophers' objection asserts that God's existence is identical to His essence, yet we conceive Him without grasping His quiddity. Does this intellectual limitation humble you when you approach God in worship? How might your prayer change if you truly accepted that God's reality exceeds every concept you can formulate?

Razi's Reply: Defending the Additionalness of Existence
Razi provides a composite answer to all three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a self-evident principle and careful distinctions to refute all three objections.

Core Idea
Razi's answer pivots on the impossibility of contradiction and the distinction between real and mental attributes.

Having presented the objections, Razi now systematically dismantles them. His answer is not merely defensive but reveals deeper principles about knowledge, existence, and the structure of reality. This lesson walks through his elegant refutation, showing how each objection collapses once we apply clear thinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the full Arabic answer and translation. Notice how Razi begins with a strong self-evident premise.
The answer is structured: first a general principle, then responses to each objection.
2
Understand the self-evident principle
Razi states: If one thing is known and another unknown, they cannot be identical. This is because a single thing cannot be both known and unknown simultaneously.
This principle directly refutes the first objection about God's existence.
3
Follow Razi's response to the second objection
Razi clarifies that 'assent that existence has occurred' does not mean existence gets another existence, but rather that existence attaches to quiddity. This avoids regress.
This is exactly the original proof: existence is additional to quiddity.
4
Examine the responses to the third objection
For the relations objection, Razi says these are mental considerations, not real additions. For mental existence, he argues that quiddity exists in the mind without external existence, and externally without mental existence, showing they are distinct.
This distinction preserves the proof for external existence.

Source Text

المسألة الثانية
فإن قيل : هذه حجة منقوضة على قول الفلاسفة؛ فإن عندهم وجود الباري سبحانه نفس حقيقته، مع أنا نعقل وجوده ولا نعقل ماهيته.
وأيضًا فإنا نتصور أن الوجود ما هو ؟ ثم نصدق بأن ذلك المفهوم حاصل وكائن واقع والتصور في التصديق فيلزم أن يكون تصديقنا بأن الوجود واقع وحاصل دالا على حصول وجود، ويلزم منه التسلسل
وأيضًا: فإنا قد نعقل ذاتين مع الذهول عن كون أحدهما لازما للآخر، أو ملزوما له، أو كون أحدهما مؤثرًا في الآخر، أو أثرًا له، أو كون أحدهما حالا في الآخر، أو محلا له فيلزم أن يكون كون الشيء لازما للآخر وملزوما له، ومؤثرًا فيه وأثرًا له، وحالا فيه ومحلا له زائدًا على الذات، وذلك محال لإفضاء ذلك إلى التسلسل، وأيضًا فهب أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن الوجود الخارجي زائد على الماهية ولكنه لا يفيد أن الوجود الذهني زائد على الماهية.

The Second Issue. If it is said: This argument is refuted according to the philosophers; for with them the existence of the Exalted Creator is identical to His essence, while we conceive His existence and do not conceive His quiddity. Also, we conceive what existence is, then we assent that that concept is actual, occurring, and real, and conception is prior to assent, so our assent that existence is actual and occurring must indicate the obtaining of existence, which leads to infinite regress. Also, even if what you mention indicates that external existence is additional to quiddity, it does not show that mental existence is additional to quiddity. The answer: If one of two things is characterized as known and the other as unknown, then if there were no difference between them, the single thing would be characterized as known and unknown, necessitating the conjunction of negation and affirmation, and that is impossible in the primality of minds. Since this premise is among the strongest self-evident truths, raising a refutation against it is skepticism, which does not deserve an answer. Also, with us, God's existence is additional to His quiddity. As for our assent that existence has occurred, its meaning is not that existence has another existence, but rather whether existence has occurred for the quiddity or not; this is precisely the proof we have adduced. As for the talk of necessity and necessitation and the like, they are mental considerations, unlike external existence, for it cannot be said that it is a mental consideration; otherwise it would follow that there is no existent in external reality, and it would be said that its existence in external reality is the very quiddity; then what you mentioned of the conjunction of negation and affirmation regarding the same thing would follow, and that is impossible. Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. Razi presents three objections against the proof that existence is additional to quiddity (essence), then gives a composite answer. The first objection: Philosophers hold that God's existence is identical to His essence, yet we conceive His existence without knowing His quiddity—implying existence is not additional. Thus the objections are refuted.

?

Opponent's Argument and Refutation on Existence as Additional to Essence
Razi presents and refutes the argument that existence is not additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that existence cannot be additional to essence and how Razi refutes it by clarifying the neutral nature of quiddity.

Core Idea
Existence is additional to quiddity without absurdity because quiddity is neutral to existence and non-existence.

This passage presents a classic objection against the Ash'ari position that existence is a distinct attribute added to essence. The opponent argues that if existence were additional, it would lead to circularity or absurd inherence in non-existence. Razi responds by clarifying the ontological status of quiddity, showing that it is not pure non-existence but a real locus distinct from both existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Carefully read the first part of the passage where the opponent presents a dilemma: if existence is additional, its subsistence in quiddity either depends on quiddity being existent (leading to self-conditioning or regress) or does not (leading to inherence in pure non-existence).
Notice the two horns of the dilemma and the absurd consequence of the second horn.
2
Understand the absurd consequence
The opponent argues that if an existent attribute can inhere in a non-existent locus, then we can doubt whether perceived objects like a wall truly exist, because we only perceive attributes. This is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine Razi's refutation
Read Razi's answer: the locus of existence is quiddity, which in itself is neither existent nor non-existent. So existence does not inhere in pure non-existence, but in a quiddity that is a real extramental entity.
Razi distinguishes between quiddity qua quiddity and quiddity as existent or non-existent.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how Razi's response avoids both horns of the dilemma: existence does not require quiddity to already be existent, nor does it inhere in pure non-existence.
This preserves the real distinction between essence and existence without falling into absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال الوجود غير زائد على الماهية بأن قال : لو كان الوجود زائدا على الماهية لكان قيام الوجود بالماهية وإن توقف على كون الماهية موجودة لزم إما كون الشيء مشروطا بنفسه، أو وقوع التسلسل، وإن لم يتوقف عليه فحينئذ يلزم قيام الصفة الثبوتية بالعدم المحض وذلك محال؛ لأن المحسوس عندنا من الذوات ليس إلا الصفات، وإذا جوزنا قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فالجدار الذي نشاهده لا نشاهد منه إلا لونه وكثافته وثقله، فإذا لم يبعد قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمحل المعدوم، لم يبعد أن يكون الموصوف بالكثافة والثقل واللون والمخصوص معدوما محضاً، وذلك يقتضي وقوع الشك في أن ذات الجدار وذات الإنسان هل موجودة أم لا؟ ومعلوم أن ذلك فاسد.
والجواب عنه : أن محل الوجود هو الماهية ثم إن الماهية من حيث هي هي ماهية مغايرة للوجود والعدم، فلا يلزم من ذلك قيام الموجود بالمعدوم.
And one who said that existence is not additional to quiddity argued by saying: If existence were additional to quiddity, then the subsistence of existence in quiddity either depends on quiddity's being existent, whichwould entail either a thing being conditioned by itself or an infinite regress; or it does not depend on it, in which case it entails the subsistence of a positive attribute in pure non-existence, and that is impossible. For what is perceived by us of essences is only attributes; if we allow an existent attribute to subsist in a non-existent locus, then the wall we observe—we observe only its color, density, and weight. If the subsistence of an existent attribute in a non-existent locus is not improbable, then it is not improbable that the thing described by density, weight, and color, and specifically that thing, is pure non-existence. That would lead to doubt whether the essence of the wall and the essence of a human exist or not, and it is known that this is absurd. The answer to it: The locus of existence is the quiddity, and then the quiddity insofar as it is quiddity is different from existence and non-existence, so that does not entail the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer: the opponent argues that existence cannot be additional, and Razi refutes him. The opponent's argument: If existence is additional to quiddity, then existence inheres in quiddity. This inherence either requires the quiddity to already be existent (leading to self-conditioning or regress) or not (allowing a positive attribute to inhere in pure non-existence). The latter is absurd, because it would imply that perceived objects like a wall could have attributes while their essence is non-existent, leading to skepticism about whether anything exists. Razi's answer: Existence subsists in quiddity, but quiddity considered purely as quiddity is neutral to existence and non-existence—it is not non-existent in the sense of pure nothing when considered in itself. So there is no inherence in pure non-existence; the quiddity is a real locus, even if it is not yet existent in extramental reality. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قيام (qiyam)
صفة ثبوتية (sifah thubutiyyah)
عدم محض (adam mahd)
ماهية (mahiyyah)
وجود (wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is additional to quiddity, then quiddity must be non-existent before receiving existence, leading to inherence in pure non-existence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that quiddity in itself is not non-existent; it is distinct from both existence and non-existence, so existence can inhere in it without absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent argue would happen if existence is additional to quiddity and depends on quiddity being existent?
A) It would lead to a contradiction in God's attributes.
B) It would require either self-conditioning or infinite regress.
C) It would make quiddity identical to existence.
D) It would prove that existence is mental only.

Key Takeaway
Razi successfully defends the real distinction between essence and existence by showing that quiddity is a neutral locus capable of receiving existence without absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not part of your essence, but given to you by God at every moment, do you truly live with a constant sense of dependence on Him? How would your daily actions change if you felt every breath as a new gift of existence?
?
The opponent argued that an added existence leads to absurdity. Have you ever rejected a truth because you could not immediately see how it could be coherent? What would it take for you to hold firm to a belief even when it seems to raise difficult questions?
?
Razi shows that quiddity is neutral to existence. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse your identity (quiddity) with your existence, so that you feel you deserve to exist or that your existence is self-justified? How can you remind yourself that you are contingent in every moment?

Razi's Third Proof: Essences as Possible and Originated
Razi presents a logical proof for the origination of essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that essences are possible (not necessary) and therefore originated, which invalidates the view that non-existence is a thing.

Core Idea
Essences can't be necessary, so they are possible and thus created.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existents are things. In this proof, he argues that essences (the 'whatness' of things) cannot be necessary in themselves, otherwise we would have multiple necessary existents—impossible. He then reminds us that every possible is originated, so essences must be originated, undermining the idea that non-existents are eternal realities. Let's follow his logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic and then the English translation. Try to follow Razi's flow: he states his premise, draws a contradiction, and concludes.
Notice how he uses the term 'واجب الوجود'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Razi says 'if essences were necessary in themselves, then the Necessary Existent would be multiple'. Mark this conditional and its impossibility.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Trace the conclusion about possiblity
Since the conditional leads to impossibility, the antecedent is false: essences are not necessary, so they are possible (mumkin). Confirm this step.
4
Connect possiblity to origination
Razi then invokes the principle that every possible is originated (hadith). Apply that to essences to conclude they are originated.
This principle is established earlier in kalam.
5
Understand the refutation
The conclusion that essences are originated directly refutes the Mu'tazilite view that non-existents (ma'dum) are things, because if non-existents were essences, they would be originated, not eternal.
Razi says this refutes القول بأن المعدوم شيء.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا بأن هذه الماهيات ممكنة لذواتها وذلك لأنها لو كانت واجبة التقرر لذواتها في الخارج لكانت واجبة التقرر في الخارج ممتنعة الزوال، ولا معنى لواجب الوجود إلا ذلك، وحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، وذلك محال، وإذا ثبت أنها في كونها متقررة في الأعيان ممكنة وثبت أن كل ممكن محدث، وثبت أنها من حيث إنها ماهيات محدثة، وذلك يبطل القول بأن المعدوم شيء، وتمام تقرر هذا البرهان قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث الأجسام.
We only say that these essences are possible in themselves because if they were necessary in their self-constitution in external existence, they would be necessary in external existence and impossible of cessation, and there is no meaning of necessary existence except that, and then the necessary existence would be more than one, and that is impossible. And when it is established that they are, in their being constituted in external things, possible, and it is established that every possible is originated, and it is established that they are, inasmuch as they are essences, originated, that invalidates the claim that non-existence is something. And the complete establishment of this proof has preceded in the issue of the origination of bodies.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) by Razi against the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existents are things. Razi argues that essences (mahiyyat) are possible in themselves. If they were necessary in their self-constitution, they would be necessary existents, and there would be multiple necessary existents, which is impossible. Thus they are possible (mumkin). Since it is established that every possible is originated (hadith), these essences must be originated. This refutes the view that non-existence is something, because if non-existents were essences, they would be originated, not eternal. Razi notes that the full proof has been given earlier in the discussion on the origination of bodies.

Key Terms
ماهيات (essences)
ممكن (possible)
واجب الوجود (necessary existent)
محدث (originated)
معدوم (non-existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Razi is arguing that essences are originated in time (i.e., created from nothing at a moment), but he means they are dependent and possible, not eternal and self-sufficient. He is refuting the idea that non-existents have a kind of eternal reality.
Correction: Razi's 'originated' here means that these essences are not eternal; they come into existence when Allah creates them. The argument establishes their ontological dependence, not necessarily their temporal beginning.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue would happen if essences were necessary in themselves?
A) They would be eternal.
B) There would be multiple necessary existents.
C) They would be non-existent.
D) They would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that essences cannot be necessary, so they are possible and hence originated, refuting the notion that non-existence has any reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every essence is possible and dependent on the Necessary Existent, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you living as though you are self-sufficient, or as someone sustained moment by moment? What would change if you truly believed that you are not necessary — that without God you would be nothing?
?
Razi shows that assuming necessity for anything other than God leads to contradiction. Where in your life have you made something 'necessary' for your happiness or security — a person, a job, a possession? How can you dismantle that false necessity and place your reliance solely on God?
?
The proof concludes that non-existence is not a thing. Do you fear non-existence? Death? Annihilation? How does knowing that only God is necessary, and everything else is possible and originated, reshape your perspective on your own mortality and the fleeting nature of this world?

Razi's Refutation: Power Relates to Existence, Not Quiddity
Razi refutes the second proof by analyzing the meaning of 'maqdūr'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that non-existent possibles can be distinguished because they are objects of divine power.

Core Idea
Power relates to existence, not to quiddities fixed in non-existence.

In this passage, Imam Razi confronts an argument that non-existent possibles are distinguished because they are within God's power. He carefully defines what it means for something to be 'within power' and shows that the opponent's own assumptions lead to a contradiction. By the end, you will see why the argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that non-existent possibles are distinguished because some are within divine power (maqdūr) and others are not. This distinction must occur before existence.
This is the second proof for the distinction of non-existents.
2
Focus on Razi's definition of 'maqdūr'
Razi defines 'maqdūr' as that which the powerful agent can actualize (taḥqīq) or nullify (ibṭāl). This is key to his refutation.
Note that the agent has direct effect only on existence, not on the quiddity itself.
3
Note the opponent's own view on quiddities
The opponent holds that quiddities (māhiyyāt) are fixed in non-existence, and the agent only bestows the attribute of existence upon them. The agent does not affect the quiddity itself.
This is accepted by the opponent, so it becomes a premise in Razi's argument.
4
Follow Razi's deduction
If the agent's effect is only on existence, then 'what is within power' must be either existence itself or the quiddity's being qualified by existence. In either case, it is not present in the state of non-existence.
Thus, whatever is an object of power cannot be said to be established in non-existence.
5
Conclude the dilemma
Razi concludes that the opponent's claim leads to a contradiction: what can be an object of power cannot be fixed in non-existence, and vice versa. Therefore, the original proof fails.
The specious argument (shubha) has fallen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دليلهم الثاني : على قولهم إن المعدوم متميز، وهو أن المقدور قبل دخوله في الوجود متميز عن غير المقدور، وذلك يقتضي وقوع الامتياز في المعدومات. فالجواب : أن المقدور هو الذي يكون للقادر فيه تأثير إما بالتحقيق وإما بالإبطال وعندكم هذه الماهيات ثابتة في العدم، ولا تأثير للقادر فيها البتة، وإنما تأثير القادر في إعطاء صفة الوجود لتلك الماهيات، فالمقدور إما الوجود وإما موصوفية الماهية بالوجود، وأيا ما كان فهو غير ثابت حال العدم، فثبت أن الذي يمكن جعله مقدورًا استحال القول بكونه ثابتا في العدم والذي يمكن القول بكونه ثابتا في العدم استحال القول بكونه مقدورًا للقادر، فقد سقطت هذه الشبهة.
As for their second proof—for their claim that the non-existent is distinguished—it is that what is within power before its entry into existence is distinguished from what is not within power, and that necessitates the occurrence of distinction among non-existents. The answer: what is within power is that concerning which the powerful agent has an effect, either by actualizing or by nullifying. According to you (the opponent), these quiddities are established in non-existence, and the powerful agent has absolutely no effect on them; rather, the agent's effect is in bestowing the attribute of existence upon those quiddities. Therefore what is within power is either existence or the quiddity's being qualified by existence, and whichever it is, it is not established in the state of non-existence. So it is established that what can be made an object of power is impossible to say it is established in non-existence, and what can be said to be established in non-existence is impossible to say it is an object of power for the agent. Thus this specious argument has fallen.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that the non-existent is distinguished (mumtāz) because objects of power (maqdūrāt) are distinguished before existence. Razi responds by analyzing what 'within power' means: the agent (fā‘il) can either bring into existence or annihilate. But the opponent holds that quiddities (māhiyyāt) are fixed in non-existence, and the agent does not affect them; the agent only gives the attribute of existence to those quiddities. Therefore, the object of power is either existence itself or the quiddity's being qualified by existence—both are not present in non-existence. Hence, whatever can be an object of power cannot be statable in non-existence, and vice versa. The argument thus collapses because it wrongly assumes that the quiddity as an object of power is fixed in non-existence.

Key Terms
مقدور (maqdūr)
قادر (qādir)
ماهية (māhiyya)
وجود (wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that what is within power (like a possible thing) must have some reality before existence, but Razi shows that power relates to existence, not to the quiddity.
Correction: Razi's analysis shows that the object of power is existence itself, not the quiddity fixed in non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the object of the powerful agent's effect?
A) The quiddity itself in non-existence.
B) Existence or the quiddity's being qualified by existence.
C) The distinction between non-existents.
D) The attribute of possibility.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the distinction argument by showing that power relates to existence, not to quiddities fixed in non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very quiddity is nothing without existence, and existence is a gift from God every moment, why do you still act as if you are self-sufficient and independent?
?
Razi shows that what is within God's power is existence itself, not your essence. Are you more attached to your own 'potential' or to the actual existence God gives you right now?
?
If you treat your own plans and desires as already real in your mind, are you not making the same mistake as those who think quiddities are fixed in non-existence? How can you surrender your imaginary 'fixed' self to God's actualizing power?

Razi's Refutation of Possibility as a Pre-Existent Attribute
Razi dismisses a claim by referencing an earlier discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi dismisses the argument that possibility is an existing attribute before creation by cross-referencing an earlier proof.

Core Idea
A brief refutation can be powerful when it points to established proofs.

In this passage, Razi confronts a third argument that claims possibility is an actual attribute existing before a thing comes into being. Instead of repeating detailed reasoning, he confidently directs the reader to an earlier discussion on the world's temporality. This teaches us that theological arguments often build on prior foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice its brevity and the reference to a previous topic.
The passage is only two sentences.
2
Identify the argument being refuted
The third argument claims 'possibility is an existing attribute (ṣifat mawjūda) that occurs before existence, so the quiddity (māhīya) must be realized before existence.' Grasp what this claim implies.
3
Understand Razi's answer
Razi says the answer 'has already preceded in the question of the temporality of the world.' He is not providing new reasoning but relying on an earlier proof.
This shows the interconnectedness of kalam arguments.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why Razi uses cross-referencing. It avoids repetition and emphasizes that the earlier refutation is sufficient.
This is a common technique in scholarly works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : الإمكان صفة موجودة حاصلة قبل الوجود فوجب تحقق الماهية قبل الوجود. فجوابها : قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث العالم.
As for the third argument—namely their statement: 'Possibility is an existing attribute that occurs before existence, so the quiddity must be realized before existence.' The answer: it has already preceded in the question of the temporality of the world.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of the argument that possibility is an existing positive attribute (ṣifat mawjūda) that occurs before existence, which would require the quiddity to be realized in non-existence. Razi simply refers back to a previous discussion in the section on the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-‘ālam). He does not repeat the argument here, indicating that the refutation is already given in that earlier context. This shows his method of cross-referencing to avoid redundancy.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
صفة موجودة (ṣifat mawjūda)
حدوث العالم (ḥudūth al-‘ālam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the third argument is left unanswered or that Razi fails to address it.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that the answer has already been given in the earlier discussion on the world's temporality, so the argument is indeed refuted.

Checkpoint
Where does Razi direct the reader for the refutation of the third argument?
A) To the discussion on divine attributes.
B) To the question of the world's temporality.
C) To the proof of the creator.
D) To the section on the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the claim that possibility is a pre-existing attribute by referencing an earlier proof, demonstrating the interconnected structure of theological argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a brief answer like Razi's, do you instinctively assume it is weak or evasive? What does this reveal about your own intellectual habits, and how can you cultivate humility to trust established knowledge?
?
Razi efficiently refutes a claim by referencing prior proof rather than starting over. In your own life, do you build on previous understanding, or do you constantly reinvent the wheel? What is one area where you can stop re-arguing and rely on what you already know to be true?
?
Consider the interconnectedness of Razi's arguments. Are your own beliefs about God and existence a consistent system, or are they isolated fragments that might contradict one another? What step will you take today to ensure your faith is built on a coherent foundation?

Six Ways to Prove the Creator
Razi classifies six ways to prove the Creator and begins the first way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies six distinct rational arguments for the existence of the Creator based on different sources of need and loci of analysis.

Core Idea
There are at least six valid rational arguments for God's existence, not just one.

In the fourth preliminary of his Arba'in, Imam Razi teaches us that proving the Creator is not limited to a single method. He presents a combinatorial schema: three ways of understanding the world's need for a cause, considered in two aspects of creatures (their essences or attributes), yield six distinct proofs. This lesson unpacks that classification and the beginning of the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice how Razi sets up the classification: the only path to proving the Creator is through the need of sensible things for a non-sensible cause.
The text is cut off at the end, but the classification is complete.
2
Identify the three sources of need
Find the three proposed sources of the world's need: imkan (possibility), huduth (origination), or both combined. Understand each term briefly.
These are the different ways contingency is defined.
3
Identify the two loci of analysis
Notice that these sources are considered either in the essences (dhawat) or in the attributes (sifat), or both, or vice versa. This yields the total of six ways.
The 'both' and 'vice versa' expand the loci but the total remains six.
4
See the combinatorial outcome
Map out the combination: 3 sources × 2 primary loci = 6 ways. Razi says 'fa al-majmou' turuq sittah' – thus the total is six ways.
Some commentators explain the 'both' and 'vice versa' as additional permutations still totaling six.
5
Examine the first way
Read the beginning of the first way: proving the Necessary Existent through the imkan of essences. Notice the argument starts with the reality of quiddities and their susceptibility to non-existence.
The argument is incomplete in the text, but the structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الرابعة : أن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع تعالى ليس إلا احتياج أجسام هذه الموجودات المحسوسة إلى موجود آخر غير محسوس، ومنشأ تلك الحاجة على قول بعضهم هو الإمكان، وعلى قول آخرين هو الحدوث، وعلى قول ثالث هو مجموع الإمكان والحدوث، ثم هذه الأمور الثلاثة إما أن تعتبر في الذوات أو في الصفات أو في مجموعها أو بالعكس، فالمجموع طرق ستة : الطريق الأول : الاستدلال على وجود واجب الوجود بإمكان الذوات، فنقول : لا شك أن الحقائق والماهيات موجودة، وكل موجود فإما أن تكون حقيقة قابلة للعدم أو لا تكون كذلك، فإن لم تقبل حقيقة العدم لـ لما هي هي، كان
The Fourth Preliminary: The path to proving the Exalted Creator is nothing but the need of the bodies of these sensible existents for another non-sensible existent. The source of that need, according to some, is imkan, according to others it is huduth, and according to a third it is the combination of imkan and huduth. Then these three matters are considered either in the essences (dhawat), or in the attributes (sifat), or in both, or vice versa. So the total is six ways. The first way: Proving the existence of the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) by the imkan of essences. We say: There is no doubt that realities and quiddities exist. Every existent is either a reality that is susceptible to non-existence or not. If it does not admit non-existence for [that which it is], then it is...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth preliminary, where Razi explains that the only way to prove the existence of the Creator is through the observed need of sensible bodies for a non-sensible cause. He then notes three possible sources of that need: imkan (possibility), huduth (origination), or both. These three sources are then applied either to the essences (dhawat), to the attributes (sifat), to both, or conversely (i.e., considering the essence's attribute relation). This yields six distinct arguments. Razi then begins the first way: proving the existence of the Necessary Existent through the imkan of essences. He states that we know there are realities and quiddities. Every existent either can cease to exist or cannot. If it cannot cease to exist, then it is necessary. But the text cuts off before completing the argument. This classification, however, is complete: six ways based on combinations of three sources and two loci (essences, attributes, both, reciprocal).

Key Terms
الذوات (essences)
الصفات (attributes)
الاستدلال (deduction)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
الإمكان (imkan)
الحدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is only one valid way to prove the existence of God, such as the kalam cosmological argument.
Correction: Razi shows that there are at least six valid proofs based on different definitions of contingency and different aspects of created things.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how many ways are there to prove the Creator based on the combinations of sources of need and loci?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Razi systematically combines three sources of contingency with two loci of analysis to generate six distinct rational proofs for the Creator's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi presents six distinct ways to prove the Creator. Which of these ways have you personally never considered? What would it take for you to explore that proof deeply until it becomes your own conviction?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you feel more the weight of contingency (that you might never have been) or the reality of your temporal beginning (that you were created in time)? How does that feeling move you toward the Creator right now?
?
The first proof begins with the simple observation that the things around you can cease to exist. Do you truly live with the awareness that everything contingent could vanish? If you did, what would you do differently today?

advanced
8 min
The Agent's Effect on Essence and Existence
Razi refutes the claim that the agent affects only the qualification of essence with existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the divine agent affects both the essence (mahiyya) and its existence, and why the concept of 'qualifiedness' as an intermediary is logically impossible.

Core Idea
The agent's power extends to both essence and existence, not just to linking them.

In this lesson, we examine a classic theological argument from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi about the scope of the agent's effect. An opponent claims the agent only makes an essence qualified with existence. Razi refutes this by showing that such a qualification cannot be a real thing. Through three logical proofs, he demonstrates that the agent must directly cause both the essence and its existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and objection
Read the opening lines: the third issue and the objection. The objection says the agent's effect is on the 'qualifiedness' of essence with existence. Note how Razi frames the problem.
2
Follow the first proof
The first argument shows that if qualifiedness were a real independent thing, it would be an accident of essence, and its own qualifiedness would require another, leading to infinite regress. Trace the logic.
3
Examine the second proof
The second argument: if qualifiedness is a real thing, it would itself have an essence and existence, leading to infinite regress within each existent. Understand why this is impossible.
4
Understand the third proof and conclusion
The third proof: if qualifiedness is a thing with its own essence, and the agent cannot affect essences (as assumed), then the agent has no effect at all. Then read the correct conclusion: the agent affects both essence and existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة
للقادر تأثير في الماهية لامتنع أن يكون له تأثير في الوجود.
فإن قيل : تأثير القادر في كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود.
قلنا : موصوفية الماهية بالوجود يمتنع أن يكون أمراً ثابتاً، ويدل عليه وجوه. أحدها: أن اتصاف الماهية بالوجود لو كان أمرًا ثابتا مغايرا للماهية والوجود لما كان جوهرًا مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته، بل كان صفة من صفات الماهية، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف تلك الماهية بتلك الصفة زائدًا على الماهية وعلى تلك الصفة، ويلزم منه التسلسل.
وثانيها : وهو أن تلك الموصوفية تكون مساوية في الوجود لسائر الموجودات ومخالفة لها في الماهية فيلزم أن يكون لاتصاف الماهية بالوجود ماهية ووجود آخر، فيكون اتصاف ماهيتها بوجودها زائدا عليه، ويعود الكلام الأول فيه، ويلزم التسلسل، وحينئذ لا يكون الموجود الواحد موجودًا واحدا ، بل يكون موجودات غير متناهية، وذلك محال.
وثالثها وهو أن موصوفية الماهية بالوجود بتقدير أن يكون أمرًا مغايرا للماهية والوجود، فلا بد أن يكون له ماهية فإذا امتنع أن يكون للقادر في الماهيات امتنع أن يكون له تأثير في هذه الموصوفية، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون للمؤثر أثر، لا في الماهية ولا في الوجود ولا في موصوفية الماهية بالوجود وهذا نفي للتأثير والمؤثر بالكلية، وهو باطل قطعاً، بل القول الصحيح ان المؤثر كما يؤثر في جعل الماهية موجودة فكذلك يؤثر في جعل الماهية ماهية.
The Third Issue: If the agent has an effect on the essence, it would be impossible for it to have an effect on existence. If it is said: The effect of the agent is in making the essence qualified with existence. We say: The qualification of the essence with existence is impossible to be a real thing, and proofs indicate it. One of them: That the qualification of the essence with existence, if it were a real thing distinct from essence and existence, would not be an independent substance subsisting by itself, but rather a quality of the essence, and then the qualification of that essence with that quality would be additional to the essence and to that quality, leading to an infinite regress. The second: That that qualifiedness would be equal in existence to other existents and different from them in essence, so that the qualification of the essence with existence would have another essence and existence, and the qualification of its essence by its existence would be additional to it, and the same discussion would return, leading to infinite regress, and then the one existent would not be one existent but infinite existents, and that is impossible. The third: That the qualifiedness of the essence with existence, supposing it were a thing distinct from essence and existence, must have an essence; and if it is impossible for the agent to have an effect on essences, it is impossible for it to have an effect on this qualifiedness; and on this supposition there is no effect for the agent, neither in essence nor in existence nor in the qualifiedness of essence with existence, and this is a negation of effect and agent entirely, and it is absolutely false. Rather, the correct saying is that the agent, just as it affects in making the essence existent, so it affects in making the essence an essence.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the agent's effect. It begins by stating the third issue: if the agent has an effect on the essence (the quiddity), then it cannot have an effect on existence. This sets up a dichotomy. Then an objection is raised: perhaps the effect of the agent is on the qualification of essence with existence, i.e., the state of being existent. The response denies that 'qualifiedness' (mawsufiyya) is a real thing (amr thabit). Three proofs are given. The first proof shows that if it were a real thing distinct from essence and existence, it would be an accident of essence, and its attribution to essence would require another attribution, leading to infinite regress. The second proof shows that this qualifiedness would then be an existent with its own essence and existence, so each existent would contain infinite existents, which is absurd. The third proof shows that if this qualifiedness is a thing, it has its own essence, and since the agent cannot affect essences (as assumed), it cannot affect this qualifiedness, eliminating any effect whatsoever. The correct view is that the agent affects both the existence of the essence and the essence itself. So the passage defends the position that the agent's power extends to both existence and quiddity.

Key Terms
قادر
ماهية
موصوفية
تسلسل
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the agent only brings existence to a pre-existing essence, without affecting the essence itself.
Correction: This passage shows that the essence itself is also subject to the agent's effect; the agent makes the essence an essence and makes it existent.

Checkpoint
What is the correct view according to Imam Razi regarding the agent's effect?
A) The agent only affects the existence of the essence.
B) The agent only affects the qualification of essence with existence.
C) The agent affects both the essence and its existence.
D) The agent has no effect on essence or existence.

Key Takeaway
The agent’s effect encompasses both the essence and its existence; any intermediary state is philosophically untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly see Allah as the direct agent behind every attribute you possess, including your very essence? Or do you subtly assume your 'self' has an independent reality that Allah merely 'perfects'? How would your daily reliance change if you internalized that Allah brings both your existence and your quiddity into being each moment? Commit to repeating 'اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ' (Allah is the Creator of all things) when you feel a sense of self-sufficiency.
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you feel a sense of total contingency—that you exist only because Allah wills it every moment? Or do you fall into the illusion that you 'are' on your own? Consider: your essence and existence are both from Him. How can you cultivate a constant awareness of this dependency? Start your morning by saying, 'يا الله، أنت الذي جعلتني موجودًا وهذا الوجود'.
?
If the agent's effect extends even to the essence of things, then nothing in your character or identity is fixed without Him. Is there an aspect of your personality or belief that you treat as if it is 'just the way you are' and beyond change? How can you actively invite Allah to reshape even your deepest traits today?

The Method and First Proof from Possibility
Razi enumerates the six methods and presents the first proof from possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological framework for proving God's existence and the first argument based on the possibility of essences.

Core Idea
All contingent things need a Necessary Being to tilt the balance from non-existence to existence.

Razi begins by establishing that the only valid approach to proving the Creator is to show that sensible bodies require an imperceptible cause. He then lays out three possible sources of this need and how they combine to form six ways. The first of these ways—the argument from the possibility of essences—is presented in detail, demonstrating that contingent essences need a preponderating cause that must ultimately be Necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the methodological preamble
Read the first part of the passage where Razi states that the only way to prove the Creator is through the need of sensible bodies. Note the three proposed sources of need: possibility (imkan), origination (huduth), or both.
This sets up the framework for all six ways.
2
Learn the six-way classification
Razi explains that these three sources can be considered in essences, attributes, or their combination, yielding six ways. Understand this to see how the first way fits in.
Only the first way is elaborated here.
3
Follow the first proof step by step
Read the proof from possibility of essences carefully. It starts with the existence of essences, distinguishes those that can cease from those that cannot, and uses the principle of sufficient reason to deduce a preponderant cause.
Pay attention to the logical necessity of a preponderant.
4
Recognize the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress
The proof argues that if the preponderant were itself possible, the same reasoning would lead to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both impossible. This forces the conclusion of a Necessary Being.
This is a classic contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الرابعة :
أن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع تعالى ليس إلا احتياج أجسام هذه الموجودات المحسوسة إلى موجود آخر غير محسوس، ومنشأ تلك الحاجة على قول بعضهم هو الإمكان، وعلى قول آخرين هو الحدوث، وعلى قول ثالث هو مجموع الإمكان والحدوث، ثم هذه الأمور الثلاثة إما أن تعتبر في الذوات أو في الصفات أو في مجموعها أو بالعكس، فالمجموع طرق ستة : الطريق الأول : الاستدلال على وجود واجب الوجود بإمكان الذوات
،
فنقول : لا شك أن الحقائق والماهيات موجودة، وكل موجود فإما أن تكون حقيقة قابلة للعدم أو لا تكون كذلك، فإن لم تقبل حقيقة العدم لما هي هي، كان ذلك الموجود هو واجب الوجود لذاته، وهو المطلوب، فإذا كانت حقيقة قابلة للعدم فنقول كل موجود تكون حقيقته قابلة للعدم فإنه تكون نسبة حقيقته إلى الوجود والعدم على السوية، وكل ما كان كذلك لم يكن وجوده راجحًا على عدمه إلا لمرجح، وذلك المرجح لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً ، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان ممكنا عاد الكلام فيه، ويلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل وهما محالان، فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى واجب الوجود لذاته.
The Fourth Introduction: The way to prove the Maker, exalted, is only the need of the bodies of these sensible existents for another non-sensible existent. The source of that need, according to some, is possibility (al-imkan), according to others is origination (al-huduth), and according to a third view is the combination of possibility and origination. Then these three matters are either considered in the essences (al-dhawat), or in the attributes (al-sifat), or in their combination, or the opposite. So the total is six ways. The first way: Demonstrating the existence of the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud) through the possibility of essences. We say: There is no doubt that realities (haqa'iq) and essences (mahiyyat) exist. Every existent, either its reality is receptive to non-existence or not. If it does not accept non-existence by virtue of its essence, then that existent is the Necessary Being in itself, and that is the sought. If its reality is receptive to non-existence, we say: Every existent whose reality is receptive to non-existence has its reality's relation to existence and non-existence as equal. Everything that is so, its existence is not preponderant over its non-existence except by a preponderant (murajjih). That preponderant must be existent. Then if that preponderant is possible, the same discussion applies, leading either to circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), both of which are impossible. So one must end with the Necessary Being in itself.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage sets up the methodology for proving God's existence. It first states that the only way is through the need of sensible bodies for an imperceptible existent. The source of this need is debated: some say it is possibility (imkan), others say originatedness (huduth), others say both. These three can be applied to essences, attributes, or their combination, yielding six ways. Then it proceeds to the first way: the proof from possibility of essences (imkan al-dhawat). The argument is: essences exist; if an essence cannot cease to exist by its own nature, it is necessary; if it can cease, its existence and non-existence are equal; a preponderant is needed to tilt the balance; that preponderant must exist; if that preponderant is itself possible, the same reasoning applies, leading to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible; thus we must reach a necessary being that is self-necessary. This is a classic demonstration of the contingency argument.

Key Terms
صانع
إمكان
حدوث
واجب الوجود
مرجح
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument from possibility is incomplete because it doesn't consider attributes, but the proof is based solely on the essence's inherent possibility.
Correction: Razi's first way focuses on the essence itself, not its attributes. The argument holds regardless of attributes, as it relies on the essence's ability to cease to exist.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is necessary if an essence's relation to existence and non-existence is equal?
A) The essence is necessary in itself.
B) There must be a preponderant that tilts the balance.
C) The essence must be eternal.
D) The essence must be composite.

Key Takeaway
The first proof from possibility shows that contingent beings necessarily require a Necessary Being, avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every contingent being requires a preponderant to exist, what does that say about your own existence? Are you living today as if you are self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge that your very breath depends on the Necessary Being? What one concrete change would reflect that acknowledgment?
?
Razi argues that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. Think of a time when you excused a wrong action by blaming circumstances or people before you, effectively pushing responsibility backward. How does the impossibility of infinite regress call you to stop the chain and take personal accountability before the Necessary Being?
?
The proof begins with the simple admission that realities exist. Do you truly live as if the world around you is a sign pointing beyond itself, or do you get lost in the 'sensible bodies' and forget the non-sensible Maker? What will you do today to transform your view of a common object into a proof of His necessity?
